Rowan stood in line with the others, his boots scuffing the cold concrete floor of **Node-F1's processing center**. The massive base sat atop the Sierra Nevada range, its brutalist architecture carved directly into the rocky peaks. The air inside the facility was sterile, tinged faintly with oil and coolant. Overhead, bright fluorescent lights cast harsh shadows that seemed to stretch unnaturally long across the polished floor. He adjusted his chest rig, fingers brushing over the ammunition clips tightly packed for the **standard-issue MP5** slung across his back. Everything was in place, as it should be. The line shuffled ahead. Recruits wearing the same **black tank tops and Federation armbands** moved in unison, their faces fixed forward, determined, or resigned. A massive station was just ahead, its spindly mechanical arms poised like a predator waiting to strike. The **barcode station**, Rowan thought. This is where they’d brand him. Permanently. The thought sent a shiver down his spine. He didn't know that free endless benefits would turn into this very moment. He cast a glance to his right. A **combat engineer**, grizzled and indifferent, worked methodically with a handheld scanner. The green glow of the device flickered briefly as it confirmed each recruit’s mark, the faint sound of its approval after the processing. The recruits whose marks passed inspection marched forward, their necks stiff with the knowledge of what the barcode meant: **property of the Federation**, cataloged forever in the **Thunderhead Terminal** system. Ahead of him, Rowan caught sight of the **Eradicator**. The towering figure stood sentinel near the processing station, a silent monolith of power wearing a matte black exoskeleton armor, and the combative uniform underneath. The visor of his blacked-out helmet gave no indication of where his eyes rested, but Rowan felt it. That heavy, oppressive feeling of being watched, judged, measured. The line moved again. Closer now. Rowan swallowed, his throat dry. His mind drifted to the stories he'd heard, the pain of the imprinting, the sound of the machine as it carved into flesh. Some said it was quick, others claimed it lingered, but no one ever said it was easy. His fingers tightened involuntarily into a fist. He couldn’t see past the line, but the noises of the **barcode machine** was unmistakable, its mechanical grind growing louder with each step forward. A young recruit ahead of him flinched as the machine’s arms darted out to press against the back of their neck. Rowan caught a glimpse of their face as they moved forward, a blank expression, but the slight twitch of their jaw betrayed the sting. As he stepped closer, the temperature felt colder, the air heavier. He rolled his shoulders, trying to focus. **This is it. No turning back now.** A faint, bitter laugh played in the back of his mind, there hadn’t been any turning back the moment he set foot in **Node-F1**. His thoughts blurred as the mechanical arms of the barcode station came into view. All around him. Rowan stared ahead, his heartbeat loud in his ears. Another recruit passed through, and the combat engineer waved Rowan forward. One step. Then another. The line disappeared behind him as he faced the machine. His shoulders squared, and his jaw clenched. The arms hissed, unfolding toward him. **It was time.** **[[Stay Silent]]** **[[Observe the Engineer]]** **[[Check Your Equipment]]**Rowan stood motionless, his shoulders squared, his head slightly bowed, trying to block out the oppressive noise of **Node-F1's processing chamber**. The sound was unrelenting. Nearby, voices barked orders, short and curt through the monotonous din. Rowan’s eyes stayed fixed forward, unblinking. There was nothing to say. No words would matter here. He stepped forward, the soles of his boots pressing firmly against the line etched on the floor. The machine overlooked before him, larger up close, its skeletal arms gleaming in the harsh light. Its movements were almost surgical, and the air around it was cold, unnaturally so, as though the machine itself radiated the absence of warmth. "Hold still." a voice came from somewhere to his left. Rowan didn’t turn to look. The machine buzzed as it scanned him, a pair of optical sensors glowing faintly as they adjusted to his height and position. Then, with a mechanical whir, one of its arms extended, its metallic joints clicking. Rowan felt the cold steel press against the back of his neck, just below the base of his skull. The hiss of the needle was sharp, like a serpent's strike. A brief, searing pain lanced through his skin as the barcode was etched into his flesh. It wasn’t agony, but it was enough to make his muscles tense involuntarily. He kept his breathing steady, forcing himself not to move. He wondered, fleetingly, what the mark looked like, a neat arrangement of black lines and numbers. The machine’s scanner beeped, the sound through the pounding in his ears. Processed. That’s what they called it. Rowan stepped foward, his hand instinctively brushing against the back of his neck. The skin was tender, warm, but the barcode was already there, etched permanently, a part of him now. He moved to the side, falling into step with the other recruits who had already been processed. There was no ceremony to it, no acknowledgment of what had just happened. But as he walked, Rowan couldn’t shake the feeling that something fundamental had changed. It wasn’t the pain of the imprint or even the efficiency of the machine, it was the Federation’s control. For a moment, he tried to imagine a life beyond these walls, beyond the concrete and steel of Node-F1. But the thought slipped away as quickly as it came, drowned out by the noise around him and the silent march of the recruits ahead. Rowan kept walking, the barcode on his neck burning of the choice, or lack of choice, that had brought him here. **[[Move to the Next Room]]** **[[Look at the Eradicator]]**Rowan watched the combat engineer with intensity that bordered on fixation. The man, his gloved hands steady as they guided the scanner over each recruit’s neck. The faint noises of the machine was a constant drone, punctuated only by the clinical *beep* that followed each successful scan. With every sound, Rowan’s unease deepened. There was something disturbingly mechanical about the process. Each grunt stepped forward, offered their neck like livestock in a processing line, and left with a small, glowing mark that would define them from now on. No words were exchanged. No acknowledgment of individuality. He forced himself to keep moving, to suppress the instinctive tightening in his chest. His breath was shallow, his heartbeat loud enough in his ears that he half-wondered if anyone else could hear it. When the grunt ahead of him stepped away, rubbing his freshly marked neck, it was Rowan’s turn. He hesitated, just for a moment, his eyes turning to the combat engineer. The man didn’t look up, his focus entirely on the glowing scanner in his hand. Rowan willing his legs to move, and stepped into place beneath the machine. The metal arm descended with a low hiss, its cold surface barely brushing the back of his neck. Rowan flinched despite himself. He felt the faint warmth of the scanner’s glow before he heard the soft hum of the device powering up. For a moment, time seemed to stretch unbearably thin. The scanner’s glow shifted from yellow to green. *Beep.* Rowan felt the vibration of the sound more than he heard it. It was done. Just like that, the barcode was confirmed, the mark now seared into his skin. He stepped away from the machine, his movements stiff and automatic, like a marionette pulled by unseen strings. The skin of his neck tingled where the barcode had been imprinted. He resisted the urge to reach up and touch it, knowing it would only draw unwanted attention. Instead, he kept his hands at his sides and moved back into line. When he finally dared to glance up, his eyes met the blank helmet of the **Eradicator**. The towering enforcer stood motionless, his massive frame an impenetrable wall of black armor. The reflective surface of his helmet revealed nothing, not his expression, not his intent, only Rowan’s own image staring back at him. Rowan couldn’t shake the feeling that he was being weighed, measured, judged. The Eradicator tilted his head slightly, just enough to suggest an unspoken acknowledgment. Whether it was approval, disapproval, or something else entirely, Rowan couldn’t tell. He looked away quickly, his chest tightening as he fixed his eyes on the ground ahead. As he stood there, the faint sounds of the scanner behind him continued, marking the next recruit and the one after that. But Rowan barely heard it. It carried expectation, compliance, and perhaps something darker, something Rowan didn’t dare name. He straightened his shoulders, forcing himself to stand tall, even as his mind churned with unease. He was marked now. Processed. No longer just Rowan. Just another grunt. **[[Say Something to the Eradicator]]** **[[Move to the Next Room]]**Rowan takes a quick moment to check his gear. His chest rig feels tight against his torso, but everything is in place, his MP5 is secure, the magazines full, and the ammunition for the day’s operations is ready. He breathes a little easier knowing that he's prepared. The line continues to move forward, and it’s almost his turn. He'd glance over to the engineer, then to the Eradicator. The machine ahead is a blur of metallic limbs, waiting for him. His mind races with the thought of being marked, of what that barcode will mean for him. Before he can think too long on it, the grunt ahead of him steps up. The machine moves quickly. The scanner beeps, confirming the process. He is next. He'd take a deep breath and step forward. **[[Accept]]** **[[Refuse]]**The chamber they entered was immense, more factory than training hall, its sheer size overwhelming. Bright white lights stretched in perfect grids overhead, their sterile glow spilling down onto the dozens of recruits who moved like a tide across the cavernous space. Rowan couldn’t help but feel like cattle, herded into an unforgiving process he barely understood. Around him, other grunts shared the same hollow stare: faces pale, movements mechanical. The floor was marked in bright white lines, clean and perfect, dividing the room into neat formation squares. Staff sergeants paced up and down, giving orders like machine-gun fire. “**Move it! Faster! Find your marks!**” Rowan kept his head down and fell into line as directed, wedged near the far-left corner of the chamber. His square filled quickly with other recruits, some barely older than him, others older but no less nervous. Everyone avoided eye contact, staring blankly ahead. Rowan was only 21 years old at this time, he coudn't shake the feeling anybody here being younger than him here. Then, from the far end of the room, a stage rose above the formations, a platform designed to command attention. A man stepped onto it. He was tall, his combat fatigues pristine, the black of his boots polished to a mirror finish. Rowan tensed as he strode to the center, before he stopped and gripped a microphone. “**Attention, recruits!**” His voice boomed, amplified by unseen speakers. It rolled over the crowd like a wave, drowning out every sound. Rowan swallowed hard. “**You’ve been chosen, not because you’re exceptional, but because you’re needed. Each of you is a cog in the great machine of the United Federation. And like any machine, we demand efficiency, loyalty, and discipline.**” “**Some of you were drafted because you lacked direction. Others because you sought purpose. And a few of you—**” his lip curled in disdain, “**—because you dared to defy the Federation.**” “**But let me be clear: what brought you here no longer matters. What matters now is what you will become.**” “**The Federation has promised you purpose, stability, and protection. For some, that will mean pride in service. For others, redemption. But hear me now, comfort must be earned. None of you are ready. Not yet. But we will make you ready.**” “**This is not a charity. This is not a game. You are property of the Federation now, and your life will be shaped by its needs. If you survive, you will earn your place in history. If you fail, you will become a lesson for others.**” Rowan’s stomach churned at the finality in his words. He was serious. Deadly serious. Then his eye swept over his group. For a moment, he could’ve sworn he looked right at him. “**These datapads will dictate your first assignments. Each square has been designated for specific operations. Some of you will assist with infrastructure. Others will aid in maintaining federal order.**” He paused and turned, pointing a gloved hand directly at Rowan’s group. “**And you—**” Rowan’s breath caught. “**—will be tasked with assisting a designated team with gang suppression efforts in the lower districts of Vindicta City.**” Unease moved through the square. Rowan’s heart dropped into his stomach at the name. **Vindicta City.** He’d heard of it, a mega city where neon lights masked constant violence and corruption. “**You will deploy to Vindicta City as support personnel. You will patrol, assist federal soldiers, and, most importantly, remind the civilians there who holds power. This is not a glamorous assignment. It is a critical one. If you succeed, you may earn a better placement. If you fail…**” His lips curling ever so slightly into a cruel smirk. “**…you won’t return to this hall.**” Vindicta City. They were being sent straight into hell. A staff sergeant emerged from the side of the stage, datapads in hand. “**Datapads out! Memorize your deployment orders and prepare to move. Vindicta City doesn’t wait, and neither does the Federation.**” Rowan grabbed the slim, metallic datapad handed to him, its smooth surface cold to the touch. The screen flickered to life, displaying a grim dossier: sector designations, deployment schedules, and a breakdown of the gangs that plagued the city. “**Fifteen minutes to transport! Pack light and move fast!**” the sergeant barked again. “**Gear up and report to the boarding zone. You’re Federation property now, and property doesn’t get breaks. Move out!**” **[[Review the Datapad]]** Rowan can’t help but glance at the Eradicator. And for a moment, it feels like time slows. Their mechanical exoskeleton hums softly as they shift their weight, the faintest twitch of their head signaling that they noticed him. A cold chill runs down Rowans spine as he lock eyes with the eradicator. Without a word, the Eradicator’s gloved hand moves swiftly to the weapon at their side, a 1010X pulse shotgun. The weapon is drawn in an instant, the barrel aimed directly at his head. The metallic click of the shotgun sends a shockwave of fear through his chest. Their voice is a low growl. Their posture is rigid, threatening, every inch of their form demanding compliance. ### "LOOK FORWARD!" The other grunts around Rowan remain perfectly still, their eyes fixed on the ground or ahead, avoiding any chance of drawing attention. No one wants to be next. Not after that. The Eradicator doesn’t flinch, their shotgun still trained on him. Every muscle in his body tenses, every nerve screaming at Rowan to comply. Their helmet never leaves his face, the light in it like a predator's eye, waiting for him to make the wrong move. **[[Move to the Next Room]]** **[[Stand Your Ground]]**“Excuse me, sir. I have a question.” The air seemed to freeze. Conversations and idle shuffling around him ceased as if the world collectively held its breath. Slowly, the Eradicator turned. The Eradicator’s helmet dipped slightly, its visor a reflective void that swallowed Rowan’s image. A distorted, mechanical voice rolled out, flat yet laced with an artificial edge meant to evoke something akin to anger. “**You dare speak out of turn, grunt?**” The voice didn’t belong to a man, it belonged to the machine, a modulation of disdain. His armored boots clanged against the floor as he stepped closer, his massive frame towering over Rowan like a shadow ready to consume him. “**You speak, and you invite punishment. I do not answer questions. I do not explain. I enforce. That is my purpose. Do you understand me?**” The small whine of his 1010X shotgun changing in his grip felt quiet like a warning bell. Rowan swallowed hard, the taste of fear bitter on his tongue. He nodded quickly, desperate to convey his understanding without words. For a moment, the Eradicator didn’t move, as if savoring the tension. Then, with a faint click, the towering figure straightened. “**Ask another question, and I will end your processing. Permanently.**” The final word hit like a hammer. The Eradicator turned, his attention elsewhere as though Rowan was no longer worth his notice. Around him, the line began to proceed once more. Rowan remained frozen for a moment, his hands trembling faintly. He forced himself to take a breath, to move, to step back into line as though nothing had happened. **[[Move to the Next Room]]**When Rowan’s turn arrived, the combat engineer gestured toward the machine with a curt, her visor catching the harsh glare of the overhead lights. The crimson glow of her augmented optics made her seem more mechanical than human. She didn’t speak, only tilted her head slightly, as if daring Rowan to hesitate. The air near the machine was colder, sterile, the faint scent of heated metal. As he positioned himself in front of the station, the apparatus seemed to come alive, its mechanical arms adjusting with a series of whirring motions. “Hold still, grunt. Won’t take long,” the engineer said, her voice devoid of any warmth. The brace clamped around Rowan’s neck with a pneumatic hiss, snug but firm, locking him in place. A soft noise emanated from the machine as its needle arm descended. The slim, surgical needle gleaming under the fluorescent lights. Then came the sting, a intrusive pain followed by a burning sensation that spread like molten steel beneath his skin. His muscles tensed involuntarily, but he forced himself to remain still. A low beep, signaling the completion of the mark. The brace released, retracting, leaving Rowan to take a tentative step back. He reached up instinctively to touch the fresh barcode, but the engineer spoke against Rowan. “Don’t,” she snapped, raising her handheld scanner. She passed it over the back of Rowan’s neck. The scanner emitted a crisp beep, the green light confirming his identity. “UGF.NF1.ARMY.GNT.” the engineer said, her tone dispassionate as she read the designation from her device. “Designation assigned. Welcome to Node F1. Move on.” Rowan stepped out of the station, the stinging sensation from the barcode still fresh. It felt more than physical, like the ink had seeped into his very being. He clenched his fists, his jaw tightening as he suppressed the rising unease. The faint clink of metal boots announced the approach of an Eradicator. Towering over the line of recruits, his black armor gleamed under the sterile lights, segmented plates changing with his every movement. The red slits of his helmet’s optics locked onto Rowan, studying him with unnerving focus. For a moment, Rowan thought the Eradicator would speak, but the figure simply tilted his head, as if filing away some unspoken judgment. Then, with an audible hiss, the Eradicator stepped aside. Behind him, the next grunt was already stepping forward. The line continued as if nothing had happened, but Rowan’s neck still burned. **[[Move to the Next Room]]**Rowan stood frozen, the cacophony of the processing line fading into a dull sound around him. The sweat on his back felt cold beneath his uniform, his muscles locked in defiance against the oppressive momentum of the queue. The grunt ahead of him had just stepped away, rubbing the freshly seared barcode on the back of his neck, a mark of servitude. Rowan’s boots felt as though they’d fused to the grated metal floor. He couldn’t take the next step. The combat engineer stationed by the barcode machine noticed the break in the rhythm. At first, she dismissed it, her scanner beeping steadily as she waved the next recruit forward. But when Rowan didn’t move, the sound of her device stopped, and her head turned sharply in his direction. Her approach was methodical, the gait of someone conditioned to enforce order without hesitation. The faint red dots on the visor of her helmet shined under the fluorescent lights, casting her in an almost spectral glow. Her uniform, polished black armor interlaced with reinforced combat plating, clinked faintly as she stepped closer. She stopped within arm’s reach of Rowan. Rowan could see the faint hum of the Pulse SMG she cradled, its compact structure vibrating subtly with latent energy. She studied him in silence for a moment, her head tilting slightly like a predator sizing up prey. The visor concealed her expression. “**Move up, grunt,**” she said. Rowan’s throat tightened. He didn’t trust himself to speak, didn’t trust his legs to move. The engineer didn’t blink, didn’t even shift as the silence stretched taut. Her fingers adjusted their grip on the SMG, brushing the trigger with slowness. Then came the faint *click*, a sound that seemed to be far louder in Rowan’s ears than it should have. The safety was off. “**You’re delaying the line, next step is simple. Move forward, or I’ll have to report your hesitation as defiance. And defiance has consequences.**” Behind him, the queue changed uncomfortably, but no one dared to look in his direction. Each grunt kept their head forward, rigid and silent, as if proximity alone might rope them into the standoff. The engineer leaned in closer, her voice lowering to a whisper. “**You’ve got two choices. Step up and get the barcode, or give me a reason to test this on you.**” Her tone was more disturbingly intimate, like a mother speaking to a disobedient child. “**It’s been calibrated today. No misfires.**” Rowan’s heart pounded in his chest, his body screaming at him to move, to comply, to fall back into the mechanical rhythm of the line. But his feet remained rooted, a rebellion so small yet so heavy it felt as though the entire facility pressed down on him. The engineer straightened slightly, her tone colder now, devoid of the false patience she had offered seconds earlier. “**Last warning, grunt. Move.**” **[[Accept]]** **[[Stand Your Ground]]**Rowan’s eyes were still locked onto the **Eradicator**, the towering enforcer whose presence alone could make most recruits crumble. The blackened visor of his helmet reflected Rowan’s stare, a mirror that revealed nothing but Rowan’s own defiance. Behind the Eradicator, the muzzle of a **1010X** remained steady, its cold steel barrel aimed squarely at Rowan’s skull. Still, Rowan didn’t flinch. His breathing stayed even, his hands clenched tightly at his sides but otherwise motionless. His feet planted firmly, his eyes unblinking. This was a test, he knew it, and he would not be the one to break. The Eradicator’s stance changed almost imperceptibly. His armored shoulders tensed, and a low, mechanical growl escaped the voice modulator of his helmet, a sound that sent shivers down Rowan’s spine but didn’t make him look away. The giant stepped forward, the heavy thud of his boots punctuating the silence between them. For a heartbeat, Rowan thought this was it. The trigger would be pulled, and everything would end right there. Instead, the Eradicator made a sharp gesture across the room. A nearby combat engineer approached quickly, his boots clicking against the ground as he hurried to comply. The faint glow of his handheld scanner flickered as he held it up to Rowan’s neck. A single, hollow beep confirmed the barcode’s imprint. The engineer hesitated, just for a second, glancing between Rowan and the Eradicator before tapping his comms device. His voice was quiet but urgent. **“We have a defective grunt, sir.”** Rowan heard the boots before he saw the officer. The recruits around him stiffened, their eyes fixed firmly to the floor as the officer approached. Rowan turned his head just slightly, enough to see him coming: wrapped in combat fatigues so clean they seemed unnatural in this place. A black beret sat squarely atop his head, adorned with the insignia of a ranking Federation Warrant Officer. The officer stopped in front of Rowan, pulling a **stun baton** from his belt. The weapon came alive with a sharp crackle, its tip glowing a deep, menacing red. Rowan stared at the light, hypnotized by its pulse. The officer’s voice yelled out. ### **“ARTICLE 2.2: PREPARE FOR 3 CYCLES OF RE-EDUCATION, GRUNT!”** Rowan barely registered the words before the first strike came. The baton slammed into his side with a crackling hiss, the electric shock surging through him like wildfire. Every muscle in his body seized at once, his limbs jerking violently as the pain spread from his ribs to his fingertips. He gasped, the breath driven from his lungs, but there was no time to recover. The second blow came harder, driving into his chest with enough force to make his vision blur. Pain exploded through his torso, sharp and white-hot, as though the very air around him was electrified. His knees buckled slightly, but he forced himself to stay upright, teeth gritted, breath ragged. The third strike came low, slamming into his legs and taking him off his feet completely. Rowan hit the ground hard, his knees scraping against the concrete as he crumpled forward. His hands shot out to catch himself, but the residual shock made his fingers curl uselessly. He was barely aware of the officer stepping closer, the baton arcing down. Another strike to his back. Another across his shoulder. The officer moved, delivering each blow. Rowan’s body twitched involuntarily with every impact, his nerves screaming in agony. By the seventh hit, he was barely conscious of where the pain was coming from. The eighth sent fresh agony through his ribs, and the ninth, a savage strike to his stomach, emptied his lungs completely. Rowan crumpled to the ground in a heap, his chest heaving as he tried to suck in air that wouldn’t come. His ears rang with the residual hum of the baton, his vision flickering between light and dark. He didn’t resist when two combat engineers grabbed him roughly under the arms, hauling him upright like a discarded sack of equipment. The officer looked over him. **“Get back in line, Grunt. You’re lucky it’s only 3 cycles today. If you try that again, you’ll wish you hadn’t. Next time, it’s not re-education. It’s something worse.”** The engineers dragged Rowan forward before shoving him unceremoniously back into place. His legs trembled beneath him, every muscle still spasming faintly from the shocks, but he forced himself to stand. To straighten his back. To keep his head up. Around him, the other recruits didn’t move. Didn’t look. They kept their eyes fixed forward, their shoulders tense, as though they feared the mere act of acknowledging Rowan’s punishment would make them next. Rowan swallowed hard, his throat dry and raw, and stared blankly ahead. His body ached, his vision swam, but he didn’t break. Couldn’t break. Somewhere behind him, the noises of the barcode machine started up again, its mechanical arm descending to mark the next recruit. His lungs kind of burn from the lack of real air, the smell of sterilized oxygen mixing with the scent of sweat and anxiety. The hold feels claustrophobic, as if the walls are closing in with each second. He takes a step forward, barely noticing the others as they move through the cramped space. The low buzz of the engines rattles his teeth. His fellow grunts are already packed in, all business, all silent. Black tank tops, the same armbands with the Federation seal. Each one checks and rechecks their gear, smgs meticulously locked into place. No one speaks. No one looks at each other for too long. Rowan glances around, catching the eye of one of the other grunts. The look is brief, impersonal, and it’s gone before he can even analyze it. There’s nothing here but duty, and that’s exactly how it’s supposed to be. No room for distractions. The corporal near the hatch taps on the dropship controls, his face lit up by the faint green glow of the console. He doesn’t seem to be paying attention, as if this is just another routine mission. The sergeant stands near a crate of ammo, her arms folded in front of her. Her eyes sweep the room, scanning each member of the squad, ensuring everything is in order. You never have to ask her what to do, she’ll tell you, whether you want it or not. The dropship shudders as it starts to ascend, the sound of the engines intensifying as the craft lifts off. The countdown to the drop has begun, and with it, the mission. There’s no turning back. There’s no room for hesitation. The sergeant came forward as she approaches the comms panel. Her fingers move over the controls, inputting commands. The soft crack of static fills the hold before her voice comes through. "Overwatch, we’re moving into the area of effect. Keep sightlines open and sound on. Full support if the situation turns code burnout." Her eyes narrow as she waits for a response, her fingers tapping against the panel impatiently. The response cracks back through the comms, distorted, but clear enough to be understood. The urgency in the sergeant’s posture doesn’t relax. Rowan watches her closely, noting the precise movements, the way she always seems in control. "You all know the drill. We secure the zone, clear any hostiles, and follow orders to the letter. No screw-ups. Got it? Those are our orders. Skeet has a hold on the lower districts. If we find him we kill him." A few nods, a few glances toward the floor, but no one dares to speak louder than a breath. Everyone is already focused on their roles, checking weapons and equipment, mentally preparing for what’s next. "Eyes forward. We move as one. No stragglers. You hear a shot, you hit the ground and return fire. Simple as that." The dropship rocks again, this time more violently as the exterior lights dim. His gear feels heavier now. The straps of his chest rig dig into his shoulders, a reminder of how tightly everything is bound together. The engines fades for a moment, replaced by the sound of the hydraulics releasing. The dropship’s doors slide open. The ground below is a blur of dark shapes, a patchwork of buildings and roads and scattered debris. There’s no time to think. The dropship hovers just long enough for them to deploy, and then, without ceremony, it lifts off again, leaving the squad behind. Rowan’s boots hit the ground with a thud. The dust from the landing stirs around him as he kneels into position, his rifle at the ready. The zone is theirs, for the moment. Vindicta City. The target is clear: gang control. But the real question is whether or not they’ll leave alive. This squad? They’re his team. For better or worse, they have to stick together. No matter what happens next. The distant cityscape stretches before him, a strange quiet settling over the deserted streets. Rowan keeps his pace steady, following the corporal who leads the squad. Their rifle is held loosely in their hands, but their eyes sweeps constantly. There’s no sense of urgency in the corporal’s movements. He’s calm, almost too calm. A seasoned veteran who knows better than to rush. Behind Rowan, the sergeant, her steps quiet. She’s at the rear of the squad, keeping the team in check, watching for any signs of complacency. Rowan can feel her eyes constantly looking from side to side. As the squad moves through the city’s abandoned streets, there’s an eerie calm to it all. The grunts around Rowan seem absorbed in their own thoughts, silently running drills through their heads. There’s no chatter, no idle conversation. Just the sound of boots. “You good?” The corporal asks, his eyes looking briefly over his shoulder to look at Rowan. Rowan nods. The place feels wrong, like it’s a ticking time bomb waiting to go off. The quiet is suffocating. It's almost too quiet. The streets are littered with debris, the city’s decay in every corner. As they pass, Rowan notices the flicker of an old holographic ad on the wall, half burnt out, the faded image of a forgotten brand flashing in and out of existence. Rowan’s eyes dart briefly to the other grunts around him. He catches sight of one in particular, a grunt with dark curly hair tied back in a bun. But what catches Rowan’s eye is the intricate cyberware lining her arms and face. The glimmer of metal beneath her skin is subtle, almost like it’s part of her, but it’s clear she’s been augmented. Her arms shimmer with faint circuitry, and the lines of her face carry the same, almost delicate, metallic pattern. Her eyes look for a moment, glancing at Rowan, and their eyes meet. His heart skips a beat, and for a second, he can’t help but notice how pretty she is, how strange and beautiful her mix of cyberware and flesh looks. It’s almost like the cybernetics are enhancing her, making her seem even more... alive. There's something about her, something Rowan can’t quite put his finger on. Perhaps she was just too pretty to be fighting a war. But he quickly looks away, his self-confidence all but evaporated. He had wanted to say something, to compliment her, but the thought of doing so paralyzes him. What if it comes off wrong? What if the sergeant hears? What if he’s reported for it? The thought of the consequences, Cycles, Pain. Possibly even worse. It’s safer to just keep his thoughts to himself. Instead, Rowan focuses on the task at hand, reminding himself to stay alert. The city doesn’t forgive mistakes. As they round another corner, the corporal motions for them to stop, his hand rising sharply in a signal that commands instant obedience. The sergeant’s voice cracks through the comms. “We’re moving in. Secure the area. Stay in formation.” The squad halts, falling into a tighter formation. Rowan’s grip tightens on his MP5, the tension settling into his bones. It’s not just a routine patrol anymore. Something’s off. He can feel it. The adrenaline begins to trickle in, the anticipation hanging thick in the air as the squad prepares for what comes next. Whatever it is, it’s about to get real. He couldn’t help but notice the way she held herself, poised, confident, utterly in control. The kind of leader anyone would want to follow, anyone would want to impress. His focus was forward now, the alley stretching out like the maw of some great beast, dark and silent except for the distant drip of water and the rustle of unseen trash. He slowed his breathing. The grunt beside him moved with less confidence. Rowan winced at the sound, already forming a mental note to berate him later, assuming they both came out of this in one piece. Every instinct screamed that they weren’t alone in the darkness ahead. Rowan’s thumb hovered over the safety of his weapon, ready to switch it off at the slightest hint of movement. His mind raced. If something was there, he needed to act fast. Not just for his squad or himself, though that was reason enough, but for her. Would she see him as dependable? Someone worth noticing? The thought fueled his steps even as the dark seemed to press in tighter. His voice was barely above a whisper. “**Cover me. I’ll check the corner.**” The grunt hesitated, clearly uneasy, but nodded. Rowan edged forward alone, his pulse thundering in his ears. Whatever lay beyond that corner, he knew one thing for certain: this was his chance. And he wasn’t about to waste it. Raines** **Former Federal Infantry** “She’s a federal transfer.” the medic states, their tone clinical, almost indifferent. “Grunt status confirmed. Your stomach churns as you watch them zip the bag closed. [[Return To Squad]]The sharp whine of a VTOL’s thrusters pierced the smoke-choked air, its sleek, angular body descending onto the asphalt just outside of the Alley. The gust from its landing sent debris scattering, clearing a brief pocket of visibility. The Federation’s emblem, and a red cross framed in polished metallic plating. Out of the VTOL emerged the Medical Corps. Each figure carried a streamlined medkit secured to their back, their helmets emitting faint glows as real-time HUDs scanned the area. The medics began to move out, weaving through the debris as their drones flitted around them. They paused only to assess injuries. Rowan’s head snapped toward the sergeant, whose urgent gestures directed the medics to his position. Two broke away from the group and closed in. Kneeling beside him and his partner, they activated their scanners, beams of light sweeping across both soldiers. “Grunt’s stable,” one announced after a brief pause, nodding toward Rowan’s partner. “Thanks to him,” they added, glancing at Rowan with a faint note of approval. Hold still.” Pain flared through Rowan’s body as he adjusted his position slightly, but he obeyed without complaint. One of the medics deployed their toolkit, a compact device unfolding into a hovering drone. The machine’s mechanical arms extended, a soft blue light pulsing from its core as it scanned Rowan’s battered look. The medic pulled out a sleek device, its nozzle resembling the barrel of a gun, and sprayed a fine layer of nanofibers over the gash along Rowan’s side. The shimmering material adhered instantly, weaving itself into his torn tissue like living threads. The sensation was strange, neither painful nor pleasant, but Rowan could feel the pressure ease as the fibers reinforced his ribcage. “Nanofiber’s holding.” the medic said, nodding to their colleague. Next came the biofoam injection. The second medic warned him briefly, “You’re gonna feel a pinch.” Rowan gritted his teeth as the syringe pressed against his side, delivering a warmth that quickly spread through his torso. He felt the foam expand internally, sealing ruptured vessels and numbing the deep ache. The drone positioned a compact exo-brace over Rowan’s chest. With a quiet *click*, the brace locked into place, stabilizing his torso to prevent further strain. The relief was immediate, though the stiffness reminded Rowan of the limitations it imposed. Nearby, his partner received her own treatment. One of the medics affixed a round adhesive patch to the shoulder wound, the material glowing faintly as it delivered a cocktail of anesthetics and regenerative pulses. She winced briefly before relaxing. The medics stepped back, their scanners emitting a final confirmation beep. “Both stable,” one said, their voice brisk but tinged with reassurance. “You’ll need proper recovery time, but you’re good to move.” Rowan staggered to his feet, his partner leaning on him for support. She squeezed his shoulder lightly, her eyes meeting his. “Thanks,” she said softly, her words laden with gratitude. “Really. I wouldn’t be here if it weren’t for you.” Rowan opened his mouth to respond, but the moment was cut short by the sergeant’s voice, booming above the hum of the VTOL’s thrusters. “Get moving, soldiers! We’re not done yet.” The medics retreated, the VTOL’s enginescame to life as it prepared to ascend. Rowan tightened his grip on his partner’s arm, steadying her as they rejoined the line. There was no time to dwell on gratitude or relief; the mission wasn’t over, and the battlefield demanded their focus once more. [[Get Back To Patrolling]]The sounds of the firefight had long faded into the background. Rowan could still feel the presence of his partner beside him. It wasn’t until now, when the immediate danger had passed and the adrenaline had started to ebb away, that Rowan really noticed her. But it was her eyes that drew him in. They were intense, a guarded sort of eyes that didn’t let anything slip through, but there was something alive behind them. Something real. Rowan tried to focus, his eyes focusing to the street ahead, as the reality of their situation returned to the forefront of his mind. Yet, for some reason, the more he tried to focus on the task at hand, the more his thoughts scattered. The realization hit him like a sudden wave, the woman beside him was *stunning*, and it threw him off balance in a way he wasn’t prepared for. His mind stumbled for a moment, and before he could stop himself, he felt the heat creeping up the back of his neck. His heart picked up pace, thumping like he’d just sprinted across the battlefield. And of course, she noticed. "You good over there?" Her voice was teasing, but there was a curious tone to it, as though she knew exactly what was going on. "Yeah. Fine," Rowan said, fighting to sound casual, to push away the awkwardness that threatened to overwhelm him. "Just, uh, keeping my eyes open. You know how it is." Her smirk widened, just enough to make Rowan’s chest tighten. The amusement in her eyes made it clear she knew exactly what was happening. "Uh-huh," she said, drawing out the word like she had figured him out completely. Her eyes remained on him for a moment longer, studying him, before she fixed her attention to the street ahead again. Rowan opened his mouth, about to say something, anything, to change the subject, but before he could, she spoke again. It was quieter this time, thoughtful. "You’re probably wondering what someone like me is doing here." The words seemed to stick between them, and Rowan glanced over at her, curiosity piqued. He hesitated just a little, because at first he didn't really care, but curiosity and boredom got the better of him. "It did cross my mind, actually. You don’t exactly scream ‘rookie.’" "Trust me, I’m no special case. Just a regular federal soldier. Was, anyway." There was something in the way she said it that made Rowan glance at her again. This time, her smirk was gone. Her eyes were fixed firmly on the road ahead. "What happened?" he asked, unable to keep the concern from slipping into his voice. She sighed deeply, the sound heavy, as though she were releasing something more than just air. "Messed up," she said simply, her voice flat but laced with a quiet bitterness. "Thought I could outsmart the brass. Took some shortcuts I shouldn’t have. They don’t like that, in case you hadn’t guessed. So now, here I am. Bottom of the barrel. A grunt. Guess it’s what I deserve." Rowan didn’t know how to respond. Her words weren’t the kind of story he had expected. There was an openness to them. But even then, he could tell she wasn’t saying everything. He could see the sorrow behind her eyes, the layers that she had yet to share. "Getting thrown down to grunt status isn’t exactly a slap on the wrist," he said carefully, his voice softening. "That’s… pretty serious." She shrugged, the motion almost mechanical, as if her own past had long since made such things feel irrelevant. "Yeah. Well, you screw up bad enough, they make sure you know it. But I’m still here, aren’t I? Still fighting." She’d been through hell, and yet, she was still standing, still pushing forward. A part of him couldn’t help but feel a deep sense of admiration, but there was something else there too, something he couldn’t quite name. "And what about you?" she asked, her voice light but curious. "What’s your story, mystery man?" Rowan chuckled nervously, scratching the back of his neck. "Nothing as exciting as yours. I was put here when they found out how fit I was... I was a hunter." She laughed, the sound a soft, genuine thing that sent a strange flutter through him. "Well, you’re doing a good job so far. Saved me back there." She nudged his shoulder playfully, the small contact sending a jolt through him. "Just doing my job," Rowan mumbled, trying to look anywhere but at her. Her eyesstill fixed on him, piercing and perceptive, as if she were trying to read something deep within him. Then, without warning, she spoke again, her voice softer now, tinged with something that might have been warmth. "You’re not bad, you know. For a grunt." It was a simple comment, but the way she said it, the tiniest hint of affection behind her words, sent a strange tightness through Rowan’s chest. He looked at her, and for a moment, their eyes met, and the world around them seemed to slip away, just for the briefest of moments. But then, just as quickly, she broke the moment with a teasing grin. "Just don’t let it go to your head, hero. We’ve still got a long patrol ahead. Names Maria." Rowan let out a nervous laugh, shaking his head as he regained his composure. "Wouldn’t dream of it. I'm Rowan, pleasure to be working with you." But as your team breached, weapons raised, you froze. There, in the center of the room, stood Skeet, but something was wrong. Skeet wasn’t alone. The room was filled with more than you expected. A dark silhouette over the scene, Void Special Forces. ''It was VEGA.'' And standing beside him was Astraeus, the Void operative whose very presence felt unnatural. You didn’t need to ask questions; you could see it in their faces. The squad was stunned, your breath caught in your throat as you tried to process what you were seeing. Skeet, who had been a nightmare to everyone who crossed him, was lying crumpled on the ground. But the worst part was what was happening next. Astraeus stood up, a sickening, smear of Skeet’s remains on his mandibles. He wiped his mouth casually, as if it were just another meal, and met your squad’s stunned looks. (text-colour:#ae3ec9)[“Well, looks like you made it!”] Astraeus said with a grin, his voice almost casual, as if it weren’t a room filled with blood already. (text-colour:#ae3ec9)[“Don’t worry, we’ll credit Alpha Team for the job. We’re just... hungry.”] VEGA stood in the back, silent as ever. But he didn’t say a word. You could almost taste the confusion and frustration. Astraeus laughed, his 6 glowing purple eyes with a bizarre sense of amusement. (text-colour:#ae3ec9)[“Flesh Feeder’s Curse,”] he said, his voice almost lighthearted. (text-colour:#ae3ec9)[“Sucks.”] That one sentence seemed to explain everything, but it didn’t make it any easier to understand. The Void Special Forces, seemingly always one step ahead, had beaten you to the punch. They’d made their move, and taken care of business in their own way. Your squad was still trying to catch their breath, trying to wrap their heads around what had just happened. It felt like a surreal moment, a bitter taste in your mouth that only grew worse the longer you stared at the Void operatives standing in front of you. Skeet, the man who had terrorized so many, had been taken out not by you, but by them. Maria shot you a glance, her expression unreadable, but something in her eyes sparkled with a quiet understanding. You knew, in that moment, things were about to get a lot more complicated. The sergeant, ever the professional, was the first to speak after the shock wore off. “Alright, we’re pulling out, Alpha Team can take the credit for the kill.” But you couldn’t shake the uneasy feeling in your gut. You had just met the Void Special Forces face-to-face and it was clear now that the line between friend and foe wasn’t as clear-cut as you had thought. As you followed the squad out, you couldn’t help but wonder, what were the Void operatives really after, and where did your team stand in the bigger picture?? The sergeant was busy on the comms, speaking to someone on the other end, while the corporal slumped in his seat, his eyes half-closed. His body leaned against the side of the ship like it was holding on for dear life. But, your attention was drawn to Maria. She was sitting a little too still for someone who had just been through a firefight, her fingers absentmindedly tracing the edges of her smg. Her eyes were distant, like she was lost in thought, and you couldn’t help but feel the urge to break the silence. You leaned forward slightly, keeping your voice low so you didn’t disturb the others. “You doing okay?” Maria looked up at you, a fake smile. “Yeah, just… tired, you know?” You nodded. “I get it. Long missions have a way of wearing you down.” She let out a small laugh but it was dry. “I didn’t think I’d make it out of this one, honestly. It’s not like I’m new to this, but every time I think I’ve figured it out, something new hits me.” You raised an eyebrow, intrigued. “Like what?” Maria shifted in her seat, pulling her rig tighter around herself. “I joined the UFAF for a reason. I wanted to prove something, you know? To everyone who thought I was just some..” She paused, catching herself. “Some nobody.” She looked at the ground for a second, her voice quieter now. “I thought I’d show them I wasn’t worthless.” You studied her, noticing the way her shoulders tensed as if her words was almost too much to bear. You couldn’t just let her leave it at that. “And did you?” you asked, your voice genuine, not prying, just curious. She let out a long sigh, her eyes searching the dim interior of the dropship. “At first, yeah. I thought I had something to prove. But after a while, I realized I wasn’t proving anything to anyone but myself... and I still don’t feel like it’s enough. I messed up really bad. I let people die by accident. And they put me in this position. They basically told me I was worthless.” The heaviness of her words settled between you two, and you couldn’t help but feel a pang of sympathy. You’d seen soldiers struggle with their purpose, their identity, but you hadn’t expected it to come from someone like Maria. She had always seemed so strong, so capable. You leaned forward a little more, your tone soft. “Maria, I get it. But look at what you’ve done. You’ve proved a hell of a lot more than just that. I can’t even begin to imagine what it took to get here, but it’s clear you’re not worthless.” She looked up at you. Then, that small smile returned, but this time it seemed more real, like it was coming from somewhere deeper. “Thanks,” she said, the words surprisingly gentle. “I don’t hear that enough.” You smiled back, trying to shrug off that had sudden feeling on your chest. The two of you sat in silence for a moment, but it was the kind of silence that wasn’t awkward, it felt... comfortable. It was like you had just made a silent understanding between you. The hum of the engines suddenly became louder, and you could feel the dropship beginning its descent. Maria straightened up, brushing a lock of hair behind her ear as she glanced over at you, her eyes catching yours for a moment longer than usual. The moment didn’t last long, though. The ship’s landing gear thudded against the surface, jolting everyone in their seats. The doors slid open with a metallic hiss, revealing the blinding lights of the processing facility. Immediately, you were met with the hustle and bustle of a busy base. Soldiers and officers rushed around, but your eyes couldn’t help but notice the small crowd that had gathered near the landing zone. Federal Engineers came around checking the dropship for damages in the meantime. It wasn’t just a few faces, there were quite a few, and they were all looking at you and your squad with a mix of awe and respect. Word must have spread quickly, because the looks you and your team were getting weren’t just casual glances, they were full of recognition. A senior officer stepped forward, his eyes locking on you as he gave a quick nod of approval. "Impressive work out there, Soldier." he said, his voice clipped but sincere. Maria shot you a look, a slight smirk forming on her face. "Looks like we made quite the impression." You couldn’t help but chuckle. "Yeah, looks like it." The officers around you exchanged brief glances, murmuring to each other. It was clear they were impressed, but there was something else in their expressions, like they were trying to size you up. You couldn’t tell if they saw potential or just an asset they could use. Either way, it was clear that this mission had put you and your squad on the map. As you stepped off the ship and onto the tarmac, your heart raced a little faster. This wasn’t just a debrief. This was something else, something bigger. And for the first time since you joined, it felt like you were exactly where you needed to be. Maria caught your eye once more, and for a brief second, neither of you spoke. But in that moment, there was something unspoken between you, something that you both knew, that the mission wasn’t over, not yet. And as the crowd around you began to disperse, you knew that things were about to change. **[[Ceremony Sector]]**The hall was grander than you expected. Federation banners lined the walls, their crimson insignias against the black fabric that draped the room. Rows of soldiers stood at attention, their boots polished to a mirror shine, their expressions blank yet proud. You stood at ease, hands clasped behind your back, your squad arrayed beside you in a perfect line. Maria was at your right, her posture rigid, but you caught the way her fingers fidgeted ever so slightly against her thigh. You stole a glance at her, and as if sensing it, she turned her head slightly. For just a moment, your eyes met, and something passed between you. There was a warmth there, a silent understanding, before you both turned back to face the stage. The commanding officer entered, his presence alone silencing the room. Then the Captain stepped down from the podium, holding a small silver case. He approached each member of your squad, pinning medals to your uniforms. After the ceremony concluded and the crowd began to disperse, you felt a hand on your shoulder. You turned to see the Captain standing close, his expression unreadable. “Soldier, A word. In private.” Maria glanced at you, her brow furrowed slightly, but you gave her a small nod before following the Captain out of the hall. He led you down a long corridor, the clack of his boots echoing against the polished floors. Eventually, he stopped in a small, dimly lit room, his office, you presumed. The door shut behind you, and he turned to face you. “You handled yourself well out there,” he said, his tone measured. “You’ve proven yourself as a soldier.” “Thank you, sir.” You stood at attention, heart steady but cautious. Yours.” You blinked, the words catching you off guard. “Sir?” “I’ve been watching your performance. You’ve got instincts I rarely see. Preciseness. Focus, and discipline, though we’ll see how far that goes. The Federation needs soldiers like you.” Before you could respond, the door opened, and an engineer stepped in unannounced, in a gray utility jumpsuit. He carried a sleek scanner that looked almost like a weapon, and without a word, he pointed it at you. The sudden movement startled you, and your hand instinctively twitched toward where your weapon would’ve been, if you weren’t unarmed. “Stand still,” the engineer muttered, the device emitting a soft hum. “What the—” you began, but the Captain raised a hand, silencing you. “Protocol,” he said calmly. “Relax.” The scanner beeped, and a holographic display appeared in the air, showing your service records, identification, and a detailed profile. The engineer nodded, handing the Captain a smaller device connected to the scanner. The Captain studied the data with a sharp eye, his brow lifting slightly. “So, Rowan Whitlock. Serviced during the Midwest cleansing operations. Impressive. And here…” He tapped the device, scrolling through the information. “A hunter. Is that correct?” “Yes, sir,” you replied, trying to mask your discomfort as the engineer lingered, double-checking the scanner. “Before the servicing, I was a tracker and hunter in the northern territories.” The Captain chuckled softly, more impressed than amused. “That explains it. Your tactics in the field, those skills don’t come from basic training. They’re honed. And it shows.” The hologram flickered as the engineer stepped back, offering a curt nod before leaving as abruptly as he had entered. The Federation is always in need of soldiers who can lead, who can adapt, who can think. I want to enlist you for a somewhat higher position. You will be properly enlisted.” He studied you for a moment longer, then, as if remembering something, leaned forward slightly. “One more thing. Are you married? Seeing someone?” The question hit you like a curveball, unexpected and out of place. “No, sir,” you said, keeping your face neutral. “Good. Relationships complicate things. They cloud judgment. And in this line of work, distractions are dangerous. Just keep that in mind.” He straightened, gesturing toward the door. “That’ll be all for now. Dismissed.” You saluted and exited the office. The quiet hallway felt worse than before, the Captain’s words replaying in your mind. As you walked back toward your squad, Maria was waiting near the exit, her brow furrowed in curiosity. “What happened?” she asked, stepping closer. You hesitated for a moment, considering how much to share. “Just… an opportunity,” you said finally. “We’ll see what comes of it.” Maria raised an eyebrow but didn’t press further. Instead, she gave you a small smile. “Well, whatever it is, I’m sure you’ll handle it. Nothing in the space between his thoughts, nothing in his chest. The doctors didn't say much, they never did. Just a few mumbled words about "completion" and "adjustments," their voices fading into the sterile medical room. They were quick to turn away. Rowan didn't care, didn't feel anything about it. He stood up, now fully dressed in the federation's digital grey mixpat uniform. His body felt... foreign, like it wasn’t his. But he didn’t have the energy to question it.</p> <p>He walked down the hallway, gray walls. His mind didn’t ask questions, didn’t process much beyond the directive that burned quietly in the back of his skull: Serve the Federation. His thoughts were no longer his own, only a cold voice of a life he couldn’t remember.</p> <p>As he passed through the hall, he saw Maria waiting. Her eyes were red, like she'd been crying. She stood in the middle of the corridor, her hands trembling, as if she wasn’t sure if she should approach. There was something about her, a look of familiarity.</p> <p>"Rowan…" Maria’s voice tightened, her tone hesitant, almost fragile. "I… I need to tell you something. I need to confess something to you, it’s—"</p> <p>Rowan’s mind blinked, blanked. Her words faded into static as his eyes locked onto her. Who was she? A feeling of discomfort crawled through his chest, but it was distant, unreachable. The answer didn’t matter anymore.</p> <p>"Who are you?" Rowan’s voice was colder than he expected, there was nothing in them, no warmth, no recognition. Just the directive.</p> <p>Maria staggered back, confusion and hurt flooding her face. "Rowan… You’re messing with me, right? This isn’t you, this isn’t—"</p> <p>He didn't hear her. His look was distant. He felt no connection to her, no recognition of her face, her pain. All he saw was the insignia of the Federation. She wasn’t important. He wasn’t important.</p> <p>"Get back in line," Rowan ordered, his voice mechanical, distant, and firm. There was no softness left. "Grunt. You belong with the rest."</p> <p>Maria’s eyes widened, shock turning to confusion, but before she could say another word, he moved forward. She reached out to him, her trembling hand grabbing at his cheek, pulling him back into the moment.</p> <p>"Do you even remember me, Rowan?" Her voice was barely above a whisper, raw desperation.</p> <p>Rowan didn't feel her hand. He didn’t feel anything. But somehow, he knew this was important to her, this was her last attempt to cling to whatever had once been. Her hand on his cheek should have meant something, but it didn’t. All that was left was the Federation.</p> <p>Without thinking, Rowan grabbed her wrist and moved her hand away, gently but firmly, as though he were rearranging a misplaced object. "No physical touch. Get back in line."</p> <p>He turned and walked away, leaving her standing there, frozen in confusion and heartbreak. She didn’t understand, but he didn’t care. He didn’t even know who she was anymore. All he had now was the Federation’s voice in his head, a constant hum of orders.</p> <p>He moved toward the transport sector. The hallway stretched before him like an endless tunnel, and as he approached the transport bay, he could hear the whir of the ships, the hiss of pressurized air. He stood in front of the shuttle bay, staring blankly at the doors.</p> <p>But something... something gnawed at the edges of his awareness. A shift, like a piece of his mind was trying to fight its way out from underneath the weight of the Federation's influence. But the feeling was strong, quickly replaced by the familiar, mechanical voice that guided him: Prepare for transport.</p> <p>The cold, emotionless touch of the helmet awaited him.</p> <p>He stepped forward, his hand automatically reaching for the helmet on the pedestal beside him. It wasn’t just a piece of armor. It was a part of him now, a physical manifestation of his transformation. He could feel it as he lifted it. As he slipped it over his head, the world around him dulled.</p> <p>He wasn’t Rowan anymore. He was an asset, a tool. The person who had walked into this facility, confused, questioning, emotional, was gone. The helmet sealed his fate. It was complete.</p> <p>Rowan wasn’t human anymore.</p> </div> <!-- YouTube Video for Music (Hidden) --> <div class="video-container" style="display:none;"> <iframe id="music-video" src="https://www.youtube.com/embed/7VDYRN6Csew?autoplay=1&loop=1&playlist=7VDYRN6Csew&mute=0&enablejsapi=1" frameborder="0" allow="autoplay; encrypted-media" allowfullscreen></iframe> </div> </body> </html> **[[ Maria's POV ]]**<!DOCTYPE html> <html lang="en"> <head> <meta charset="UTF-8"> <meta name="viewport" content="width=device-width, initial-scale=1.0"> <title>Maria's Perspective</title> </head> <body> <div class="content"> <p>Maria was no longer in the room with Rowan. The words left her mouth, but she didn’t hear them. It was as though she had stepped outside of herself for a brief moment, only to realize that she had been the one speaking all along. Her eyes dropped to the ground, focusing on the place where her hand had reached for him. It wasn’t Rowan she was losing, it was herself. Or so she had thought.</p> <p>It didn’t hit her until the moment he turned away. She was standing there, numb. Her body wasn’t hers. Her mind was fragmented, trying to put the pieces back together. Everything felt like a dream, blurry, hollow, as though the world was suddenly too much for her to carry. The Federation had done this to her. She had lost sight of who she was, who she was supposed to be. She didn’t know if she was ever *truly* Maria anymore, or if she had become someone else, someone who followed orders.</p> <p>But there was a moment. A moment when Rowan, this boy, this stranger, had looked at her. He had seen something in her, something worth noticing. And that was everything. In that second, she knew she couldn’t let him go. The Federation’s control was suffocating, but she was *alive*, at least. She still had breath in her lungs, and it wasn’t too late to act.</p> <p>Maria’s fingers clenched around the straps of her chest rig, pulling the material tight against her chest as she adjusted the fit. It wasn’t over yet. There was something in her that was reborn when she realized it wasn’t just Rowan who needed to be freed. She was too *somehow* in the moment she had been thinking of him, she realized that she was already pulling herself out of the darkness, out from under the Thunderhead’s influence.</p> <p>She didn’t know how. She just felt it, an energy, an awakening. It wasn’t enough to be passive anymore. She wouldn’t wait. She wouldn’t follow blindly. She had a purpose *her own* purpose and it was clear as day. She was going to free him. She was going to free herself.</p> <p>Maria took a deep breath, and with it, the tension in her body slowly loosened. It wasn’t the Federation’s voice anymore that guided her steps. It was her own. The path ahead was fraught with danger, with uncertainty, but she was done being a tool. No more following orders like a pawn. If she was to go out, she was going out with a *bang*.</p> <p>She followed behind him, keeping her distance, but not too far. Her boots moved with his, though she knew they were walking towards a precipice. She had no illusions about what might happen next. But as she watched the back of his head, his gait stiff and robotic, something inside of her screamed to reach out. She wanted to speak to him again, to tell him that she had seen the truth in him, that there was something pure, untainted, waiting to be unlocked. But she stayed quiet for now. There were no words left for him; the words were for herself.</p> <p>The transport was ahead, the same sterile silence that Rowan had walked towards. She could feel the oppressiveness, the very *thing* that had suppressed her, that had forced her to live a life she didn’t recognize. But she was free now, she had set herself free. She would break him out, free him from the Federation’s claws, just like she was doing for herself unknowingly.</p> <p>As they approached the shuttle bay, Maria’s grip tightened on her MP5. Her heart was pounding. She didn’t know if this was her last day, if she was going to make it through alive, but she couldn’t stop now. She wasn’t going to stop. There was nothing left to fear.</p> <p>Maria watched Rowan as he boarded the shuttle, detached expression on his face. She didn’t know what had happened to him, what had stolen his soul, but she was going to fight for it. She was going to bring him back, no matter what. This was *their* fight now. No longer just his. No longer just hers.</p> <p>The shuttle doors slid closed, but Maria remained outside for just a moment. Her eyes reflect of herself in the glass. For the first time, she saw a *choice* in her eyes. The same reflection that had once been empty, now held something different, a light of defiance. She was free. And no matter what happened next, she wasn’t going to let anyone steal that away from her again.</p> </div> <!-- YouTube Video for Music (Hidden) --> <div class="video-container" style="display:none;"> <iframe id="music-video" src="https://www.youtube.com/embed/7VDYRN6Csew?autoplay=1&loop=1&playlist=7VDYRN6Csew&mute=0&enablejsapi=1" frameborder="0" allow="autoplay; The heavy hum of the turbines began to dull as the craft’s engines powered down, and the ramp at the rear of the vehicle slid open with a hiss. Rowan’s vision, which had been clouded for the entire ride, cleared in an instant as the technology embedded into his helmet deactivated. The world around him snapped into focus, detailed, and full of purpose. He blinked, adjusting to the sudden view. The squad began to move as one, their boots hitting the ground. The Sergeant, a tall man with a square jaw and a demeanor that matched the weight of his rank, stood still for a moment. He listened intently to his comms, the faint static of the overwatch chatter filling his earpiece. His eyes narrowed slightly as he processed the incoming feed. Rowan felt a hand at his side, pulling out the pulse rifle that had been secured to his combat harness. It was lighter than expected, a deadly weapon with a metallic sheen. The biolock system activated immediately as his fingers brushed over the rifle’s grip. A small, reassuring click. He'd been given a genetic code, encoded directly into his DNA, which unlocked the weapon's security features. The rifle was now his, responding only to his touch. The Sergeant finally spoke. “Objective confirmed. We move in. Apartment A. Clear the building, arrest or eliminate as needed. Watch for any civilian resistance. They may be armed and concealed.” As they moved, the cool night air seemed to press against his plate carrier, the sounds of the city faint in the background. His mind, sharp and alert, held only one focus: the mission. Nothing else mattered. The Thunderhead, ever present in his mind, spoke in its familiar tone. Not that anybody, nor Rowan can hear it. (text-colour:blue)[**“The true weakness in a human is their memory.”**] **[[Apartment Unit A]]**The building was eerily quiet as the squad made their way down the dark, narrow hallway of AU-A1. The Eradicator led the way. He said nothing. His large, somewhat armored form made him the ideal point man for this kind of operation. The mission was simple, investigate, assess, neutralize if necessary. The Eradicator stopped, his head tilting slightly as if listening for something. A brief, almost imperceptible motion from the Sergeant behind him indicated it was time. The door opened with a soft creak. Inside was the lobby, sparsely furnished but filled with the smell of stale air and something else, something faintly metallic. Low, buzzing lights flickered above them, casting uneven shadows across the room. A few people sat at the far end, oblivious to the sudden intrusion, chatting quietly amongst themselves. At first, they looked up, confused. Before you could make a move, the Sergeant had broken the silence. “All units, map is being displayed, proceed to priority point.” They burst into the designated area. The civilians, most of them lounging about, shifted uneasily in their seats. They were starting to realize what was happening. The Sergeant was already ahead of them, his voice snapping through like a whip. “Everyone, get on the ground! Now!” It wasn’t a suggestion. It was an order, one that left no room for argument. The squad fanned out, weapons at the ready, their eyes focusing from one person to the next. “Get down!” the Sergeant yelled again, this time with more force. A man in the corner, looking to be in his early thirties, hesitated, his eyes looking toward the window as though considering running. You caught the movement out of the corner of his eye. Before he could even think, the Sergeant’s voice went around the room again, louder, more final. “Anyone refusing to follow will be terminated under Article 3.6. Anti-Federal behavior will not be tolerated.” Anti-Federal. It was the death sentence for anyone who defied the Federation, anyone who thought they could stand against the will of the government. It didn’t matter if they were civilians or enemies, they were all subject to the same rule. The civilians had no choice now. One by one, they dropped to the floor, hands raised above their heads. The squad moved quickly, positioning themselves to cover all angles. Rowan stood at the ready, pulse rifle steady in his hands. “Secure them,” the Sergeant said. “We’re not leaving until this place is clear.” **[[Secure Older Woman]]** **[[Secure Younger Male]]**Your eyes scanning the civilians who were now lying prone on the floor. The older woman, frail and bent with age, was closest to you. She didn’t resist as you approached her, her trembling hands raised in surrender. She couldn’t move without the help of her walking stick, and you could see no threat in her eyes. The others might be dangerous, but her frailty didn’t make her a risk. “Stay down,” You said softly, but firmly. She nodded, her breathing quick and shallow. You checked her for any hidden weapons, moving with care, but it was clear she had nothing concealed. No knife, no gun. Just a frail old woman, scared out of her wits by the intrusion. Didn’t expect her to be a threat. You heard a loud bang, glanced up to see one of the grunts, a younger man, stagger backward, clutching his chest. His eyes went wide with panic as the blood seeped from his uniform. The revolver shot was clean, a hit right in the chest. His breath came in ragged gasps as he collapsed, his MP5 clattering to the floor. The Younger Male had a revolver. The second grunt wasn’t in much better shape. A shot went out, hitting him somewhere on his left side. He dropped to the ground with a grunt of pain, his weapon falling to the side. He powered up and down his shredder in the corner, the potential violence of the weapon as it came to life and powered down again was enough to make anyone think twice. The civilians didn’t dare move or speak as the Fortifier approached. With every flick of the shredder’s power, he made his presence known. The message was clear, cooperate. Then, there was the last one. [[Secure Adult Male]] You approached the younger male cautiously. The kid couldn’t have been more than nineteen. Sweat slicked his brow, his eyes darting nervously between you and the others. Your instincts prickled, there was something about the way the younger male avoided his eyes. “Don’t move,” You ordered, stepping closer. “Hands where I can see them.” The younger male didn’t respond. You caught the motion, his senses sharpening. Something wasn’t right. Rowan crouched down, his free hand moving to pat down the younger male’s side. It was quick, professional, and for a moment, Rowan thought he’d find nothing. But then his hand brushed against the telltale bump in the male’s jacket, a concealed weapon. “Gun.” Rowan muttered under his breath, his voice low but urgent. He moved to grab it, but the younger male reacted faster than he expected. In a blur, the younger male’s hand shot toward the revolver, fingers curling around the grip. Time seemed to slow as you twisted the male’s arm before the weapon could be fully drawn. There was a sickening crack as the arm was wrenched unnaturally, followed by a sharp cry of pain. The revolver clattered to the floor as you moved swiftly, slamming a booted foot into the male’s chest. You didn’t hesitate. In a single motion, he brought his pulse rifle back up. “Article 3.2 - Possession of a concealed firearm.” Before the younger male could recover, Rowan pulled the trigger. The pulse rifle let out a soft bang, the energy bolt striking cleanly. “Anti-Federal neutralized." **[[Secure The Adult Male]]**He couldn’t have been older than twenty one, though his eyes betrayed a deep fear. You crouched down beside him. His wide, panicked eyes met mine, and you could see the gears turning in his head, he was trying to decide if it was worth making a run for it. You studied him for a moment. “Stay down.” You ordered, my voice firm but not without a tinge of hesitation. I moved in close, my hand resting near his chest as I checked for weapons. I was quick but thorough, feeling for anything that might be hidden beneath his ragged clothing. There was nothing. No gun, no knife, nothing to indicate he posed any immediate danger. You checked her for any hidden weapons, moving with care, but it was clear she had nothing concealed. No knife, no gun. Just a frail old woman, scared out of her wits by the intrusion. Didn’t expect her to be a threat. You heard a loud bang, glanced up to see one of the grunts, a younger man, stagger backward, clutching his chest. His eyes went wide with panic as the blood seeped from his uniform. The revolver shot was clean, a hit right in the chest. His breath came in ragged gasps as he collapsed, his MP5 clattering to the floor. The Younger Male had a revolver. The second grunt wasn’t in much better shape. A shot went out, hitting him somewhere on his left side. He dropped to the ground with a grunt of pain, his weapon falling to the side. The Eradicator came around, closing the gap in a blur of motion, his hand landing on the grunt’s head before he pushed him down. The Eradicator shot down the Younger Male using their AA-12. "Anti-Federal is flatline." the Eradicator said flatly, his voice void of emotion. He moved on, without a second thought. You refocused on the civilians in front of me. The Fortifier was nearby.
