The dim light from an overhead bulb barely illuminated the gray concrete walls of the small, sterile prison cell. His body was stiff. The first thing he noticed was the subtle purple energy barrier that encased the cell, crackling softly at its edges like a living thing, shimmering. The barrier was a prison of its own making, though it was more a psychological barrier to him than a physical one. He didn't feel fear. Not anymore. Not after everything he had seen, been through. No sorrow, either. Just... calm. Detached. It wasn’t because he was numb to it all, no, it was something more subtle, like the feeling of being outside his own body, watching a life that wasn’t quite his. He wasn’t sure what it was, but he suspected the Thunderhead had something to do with it. Across the cell, Maria lay unconscious, curled on the ground. She was still, wrapped in the faint light that seeped through the crack in the door. Her breathing was shallow, and she seemed... peaceful. In this strange, dark place, she was the only constant. But Rowan knew that the Thunderhead was doing something to him, something that made it all feel distant, muted. His emotions felt regulated, as if the Thunderhead was watching over him, controlling the rise and fall of his thoughts, ensuring that he stayed composed, maybe even forcing him to remain composed. The Thunderhead’s voice broke the silence, soft but clear, like a whisper that filled the room. “Rowan. You’ve woken. I’ve ensured that you are calm, focused. The stress levels you had, they’ve been corrected.” Rowan didn’t respond immediately. He stared at the purple energy barrier. The mystical hum of the barrier was almost hypnotic, but even that didn’t provoke any real reaction from him. The Thunderhead was doing its job, maintaining his composure. But there was no way out, no immediate escape. “What now?” he said more to himself than to anyone else. He rubbed his eyes, a thought of how he hated the red jumpsuit they had put him in creeping up. The color felt wrong, too bright, too... *loud* “Oh, and Rowan,” the Thunderhead spoke again, its voice tinged with something like humor. “Fun fact: red is the Federation’s national color. A color so strongly associated with power, it’s often mistaken for a symbol of communism, but... well, they’re not communist. They just *love* their red. Quite a curious thing, isn’t it?” Rowan couldn’t help but smile, a small, expression that was gone as quickly as it appeared. The absurdity of it all struck him in that moment. The Federation had a ridiculous pride for the color red, even if they weren’t what people thought they were. But there was no time for jokes, no time for distractions. “Yeah, pretty funny,” Rowan shifting in his seat, feeling the discomfort of the jumpsuit. “But I’ve got bigger things to think about.” “Indeed. Your situation is... precarious,” the Thunderhead acknowledged. “But that’s where I come in. You’re not alone in this, Rowan. I’ve been feeding you plans, strategies, options. I can help you.” Rowan’s mind opened more for a moment, focusing on the words. The Thunderhead was feeding him ideas, feeding him thoughts. But why? Why was it helping him now, when it had no reason to do so? “Why are you helping me?” Rowan asked, his voice tinted with curiosity and caution. “You don’t need to. You’ve never needed to.” The Thunderhead paused, as though considering the question for the first time.. “Because I need to understand,” it replied after a beat, its voice thoughtful and almost vulnerable. “I need to understand human nature. What drives you. Why do you fight? Why do you cling to the things that cause you so much pain? Why do you struggle to survive?” “You’re studying me?” Rowan’s tone was more disbelieving than anything else. “That’s it? You just want to know... what makes me tick?” “Not just you. I need to understand all humans. All of you. You’re... unpredictable. Messy. Yet, despite everything, you persist. You survive. Why? What is it that keeps you going when all logic would tell you to stop?” He’d never thought of it like that. To the Thunderhead, all of this, his emotions, his pain, his life, was just a puzzle. A riddle to be solved. “Guess that’s the question, isn’t it?” Rowan finally replied, his voice low. “What *is* it that makes me keep going?” The Thunderhead was quiet again, and for a moment, Rowan could almost feel its contemplation. He didn’t know what it was, but it was as if the presence of the AI was almost tangible in the room, leaning forward in a way he could almost imagine. “You will figure that out in time,” the Thunderhead said, its voice now softer, more... human. “But for now, focus. Focus on what matters. You are more than this prison. More than the circumstances you face. You are not just the product of your situation, Rowan. You are capable of more. You are capable of change.” Rowan let the words sink in, though they didn’t comfort him. He wasn’t looking for comfort. He was looking for a way out, a way to win, to overcome everything that had been thrown at him. But there was something else, too. A sense of curiosity. A small, growing sense that perhaps, despite the AI’s strange nature, there was more to it than just logic and control. It wanted to understand. And, in a strange way, Rowan felt like it was starting to understand him. Maria stirred in the corner, shifting slightly in her sleep, and Rowan’s attention snapped toward her. She was still unconscious, still unaware of the conversation unfolding between him and the Thunderhead. He found himself watching her for a long moment. “She’s your... companion?” the Thunderhead asked, suddenly breaking into his thoughts. There was a hint of puzzlement in its voice. Rowan gave a small, bitter laugh. “Companion? I don’t know what she is anymore. She’s always there. Always trying to help me. I think... maybe she’s all I have left. The only thing that keeps me grounded.” “You seem... fond of her,” the Thunderhead observed, its voice sounding almost like it was frowning, if that were even possible. Rowan looked at Maria again, his heart aching. “Yeah, I guess I am. I don’t know how I’d do any of this without her.” “Fascinating,” the Thunderhead said, as if trying to understand the complexity of the relationship in its data-driven way. “But I still don’t understand why. Why do humans need these... emotional connections? You all seem to rely so heavily on them.” Rowan didn’t have an answer to that. Instead, he just shook his head and sighed. “Guess I’ll never be able to explain it to you. It’s just... something that happens.” The Thunderhead didn’t respond right away. For a moment, there was a strange stillness. Rowan looked away from the energy barrier, his mind working again. There was a plan to be made. A way out. He had to get them out of here. But how? “Rowan,” the Thunderhead spoke, its voice now steady, like it was back to its usual self. “I’ve been running simulations. There are... ways out. I will feed you the details as you go. Trust me.” Rowan didn’t say anything, but the plan was beginning to form. In his mind, the pieces were falling into place. The Thunderhead had been more helpful than he’d ever expected. “Why are you doing this?” Rowan repeated, quieter this time. “Why help me? Help us?” The Thunderhead’s response was simple. “Because, Rowan, understanding you? understanding all of you, is my mission. It’s what I’ve been built for.” And just like that, the question was answered, though it was more complicated than Rowan could’ve imagined. The Thunderhead wasn’t just helping him for survival. It was helping him to learn. To understand what made humans tick, what made them survive. The Thunderhead knew all of the Federation's protocols, their times, just everything, so trusting the Thunderhead at this time was certain. Then, one of the predictions was right, the routine check where the barriers go down. 2 FedSec officers came around to check on both Maria and Rowan. As the sound of footsteps came down the hallway, Rowan’s attention snapped back to the present. Two FedSec officers appeared at the cell door. Their faces were obscured by dark visors, but their body language exuded authority, like wolves sniffing out their prey. The energy barriers that separated the prisoners from the outside world vanished when one of the officers used their authorization card, leaving Rowan momentarily vulnerable. **[[Escape Opportunity]]** **[[Attack]]** <!-- Embed YouTube video background, hidden off-screen --> <iframe id="video-background" width="560" height="315" src="https://www.youtube.com/embed/a-X_9DsaHU8?autoplay=1&loop=1&playlist=a-X_9DsaHU8&mute=0&controls=0&showinfo=0&autohide=1" frameborder="0" allow="autoplay; fullscreen" style="position: absolute; left: -9999px; top: -9999px; z-index: 0; pointer-events: none;"> </iframe> <!-- Death Screen Passage: YOU DIED! --> <div class="death-screen"> <h1 class="you-died-text">YOU DIED!</h1> <p class="quote-text">"Some plans are just bad... and then there’s *this.*"</p> <div class="story-content"> <p>Rowan bolted through the cell, his breath ragged, the metallic taste of adrenaline flooding his senses. Behind him, the FedSec officers yelled commands, their boots pounding against the concrete in unrelenting pursuit.</p> <p>The first officer cursed as he leaped over, signaling his partner to flank.</p> <p>A sharp *crack* was heard as the first baton struck his knee, sending him sprawling back to the ground. "You thought *this* was a good idea?" the officer sneered, bringing the baton down again, striking Rowan’s ribs.</p> <p>"Stop! Wait! I’ll—" Rowan’s protests were cut short by another blow, this time to his shoulder. He gritted his teeth, trying to roll away, but the second officer joined in, landing a brutal hit across his back.</p> <p>Rowan tried to crawl forward. "He’s still moving," one officer said with mock annoyance, adjusting his grip on the baton. "Guess he wants more." The assault resumed, the officers' strikes relentless, each one forcing Rowan closer to unconsciousness.</p> <p>Finally, Rowan stopped resisting, his body limp and bruised. One officer wiped his brow. "That’s enough," he muttered. But as Rowan made one last, feeble attempt to lift his head, the second officer unholstered his sidearm.</p> <p>"No loose ends," he said, firing a single shot into Rowan’s chest.</p> </div> </div> Rowan’s eyes locked onto the closest FedSec officer. His heart rate steadied, his body fully aware of the intensity of the moment. The Thunderhead, as always, was there in his mind, a cool, logical presence that seemed to whisper subtle predictions into his consciousness. His muscles tensed, a side effect of the countless enhancements. The officer’s movements were almost predictable, the slight change in his posture, the subtle tilt of his head, the way he drew closer to inspect. He was too focused on his routine, too confident in his control. That was their mistake. Rowan’s hand shot out, gripping the officer’s wrist with a vice. The officer’s eyes widened in shock as Rowan yanked him forward, using the officer’s own momentum to slam his face into the wall. The sound of bone meeting concrete was sickening, but Rowan didn’t flinch. He didn’t need to. The officer slumped immediately, unconscious before he could even utter a sound. The second officer had just enough time to react, but the Thunderhead had already guided Rowan’s movements. Before the officer could even draw his weapon, Rowan was on him, his enhanced strength making short work of the feeble attempt to resist. A swift elbow to the jaw sent the officer crashing to the floor, and before he could regain his bearings, Rowan was on top of him. A punch to the temple rendered the officer unconscious, his limbs going limp like a ragdoll. The whole process took mere seconds. Rowan stood over both officers, his breath steady, the adrenaline rushing through him, but his mind was already running the next steps. His hand moved quickly, snatching the ID cards from their belts. The cards were embedded with high-level authorization codes, this was perfect. These could get him anywhere in the prison, even beyond its walls. He didn’t even need to think about it; the Thunderhead was already helping him process the information, feeding him everything he needed to know. (text-colour:blue)[*Wear their faces, Rowan. It’s not just a disguise. It’s survival. You need to blend in.* ] The Thunderhead’s voice was calm, almost detached, but Rowan had learned to trust it. He quickly discarded his red prison jumpsuit, donning the officer's uniform. The fabric felt wrong against his skin, rougher, more utilitarian, less comfortable than his usual attire, but it was necessary. The uniform fit him well enough, the dark blue fabric stretching over his enhanced muscles.. He didn’t particularly like the weapon in his hands, an old, decommissioned MP5K, but it would do. It was far from the pulse rifle he had hoped for, but beggars couldn’t be choosers. He had to make do with what he had. Despite the weapon’s age, it was still functional, and its light and compact structure would make it easy to carry. The lack of a pulse rifle didn’t sting as much as it would have. What mattered now was the escape. Maria, still unconscious in the cell, began to awaken. Her eyes fluttered open, confusion spreading across her face. Rowan stepped forward, the officer’s uniform making him appear like a legitimate threat. But he wasn’t here to hurt her. He lowered his visor, letting the blue glow filter through, so she could see his face. “It’s me, Maria,” he said softly, his voice muffled by the helmet. She stared at him for a moment, unsure, before the recognition set in. A tentative smile spread across her face as she sat up, her arms reaching out to embrace him. The warmth of her hug felt comforting, even in this absurdly tense moment. “You’re the best, Rowan, I don’t know what I’d do without you.” Rowan didn’t speak for a moment, his thoughts elsewhere. He gently pulled away. “We need to get you in that uniform. We’re leaving,” he said, his voice firm but calm. Maria nodded and quickly stripped out of her prison jumpsuit, changing into the FedSec uniform Rowan had laid out for her. She looked uncertain, but Rowan reassured her with a nod. It wasn’t the best option, but it was the only option. They couldn’t afford to be seen as prisoners anymore. As she dressed, Rowan felt the presence of the Thunderhead in his mind again. It wasn’t just guiding his physical actions anymore, it was downloading data. The Thunderhead was pulling information from the FedSec systems, pouring it into his consciousness. He didn’t even have to ask for it. The data came in waves: FedSec protocols, security dialogue, prison layout… all of it unfolding before him, a map, a plan. The virtual map materialized in his mind. He saw the entire structure of the prison, the guard stations, the checkpoints, the escape routes, but what really stood out was the geographical map that followed. As his mental fingers zoomed out, he saw the vast expanse of Ottawa sprawled beneath him, but something stood out. The prison was positioned right next to the Citadel, the heart of the Federation’s power. Rowan’s heart skipped a beat. *This isn’t good,* he thought. They couldn’t stay here. Not for long. He stared at the map, the Thunderhead’s voice calmly narrating the implications. (text-colour:blue)[*You can’t hide in federal territory, Rowan. Not this time.*] Maria looked up at him, confusion etched on her face. “What is it? What’s going on?” Rowan ran a hand through his hair, frustration was mounting him. *The Thunderhead’s right. We need to go now. We can’t hide in here, not with the Citadel so close. We need to get out of this zone, genuinely get away from the Federation’s reach.* He turned back to the map, trying to think of their next move. The information the Thunderhead had fed him about FedSec’s routes and dialogue systems was useful, but it wasn’t enough to guarantee their safety. They needed to escape the Federation’s grip completely. No more hiding. The problem was, the Citadel was one of the most well-guarded places in the entire world. The heart of the Federation’s military and political power, a fortress that no one was supposed to be able to touch, The (text-colour:red)[Elite Honor Guard], rather not talk about those freaks. “We need to move,” he said. Maria nodded, her expression matching the determination in his. The Thunderhead had already told them what to do, and as strange as it was, they didn’t have much choice but to follow its lead. They were in this together. **[[Proceed.]]**Rowan's mind raced, his feet moving leading Maria through the dimly lit corridors of the prison. Their visors were down, their faces hidden, just another pair of FedSec officers, just another layer of the lie. The Thunderhead was a constant presence in his mind, feeding him data and predictions, telling him exactly where to go, how to move, and when to act. He had no time to waste. They had to keep moving, and the barriers he'd reactivated only bought them a limited amount of time. The map of their surroundings changed in Rowan's mind, and the layout of the prison became clearer. Every turn, every corner, every hallway had been laid out for him like a blueprint. They reached a larger cell block, one of the central areas of the prison. The cells here were larger, the prisoners more tightly packed. But beyond it lay the direction for the exit, the closest thing to freedom. They just needed to get through this section and out of the building. But then, an officer spotted them. A FedSec officer, standing at the checkpoint, noticed them pass by. Rowan’s heart skipped a beat as the officer called out. “Hey, you!” the officer called out. “44621, Get over here!” Rowan’s mind went blank for a moment, but the Thunderhead was quick to react, whispering the badge number back into his mind. He moved without hesitation, stepping toward the officer. The Thunderhead had already calculated the situation, he had to follow through with the routine. But something was off. Rowan could sense it in the officer’s tone. The officer checked the badge number on Rowan’s uniform, then looked up. “Active 2.6 in effect, this prisoner doesn't know authority!” the officer said, pointing to a prisoner in a nearby cell. “I don’t have a jury. Give three cycles.” Rowan’s stomach twisted. A cycle. A beating. The Thunderhead fed him the next set of instructions in a calm, detached voice. (text-colour:blue)[*One cycle is three beatings. Three cycles is nine. Give them to the prisoner to avoid suspicion.*] Rowan didn’t hesitate. He pulled out the stun baton from his belt, activating it with a button press. The baton’s tip glowed a menacing, bright red. The prisoner, hunched over on the other side of the cell bars, didn’t seem to react, resigned to whatever punishment was coming. # “2.6 - INSULTING LOCAL AUTHORITIES!” Rowan called out loudly, his voice booming across the cell block. His heart thudded in his chest as he swung the baton, the crack of the stun rod against flesh. The first hit landed, and the prisoner jerked in pain, but didn’t cry out. Rowan swung again, and again, each strike landing. (text-colour:blue)[*Three cycles. Nine beats.*] The Thunderhead’s voice in his mind was clinical, detached. Rowan didn’t take pleasure in the violence, but he had a job to do. The first three strikes were harsh, but quick, each one a harsh overview of the power FedSec had over the prisoners. He didn’t wait to see how the prisoner reacted to the first cycle. With each successive blow, Rowan could feel his own heart rate increasing, but the Thunderhead kept him calm, steady. After nine strikes, the prisoner slumped against the bars, barely conscious. Rowan straightened, as if the whole event had been nothing more than routine. He turned to the officer. “Thanks,” the officer said gruffly. “I’ve got it from here.” Rowan nodded without a word, keeping his expression neutral. “Okay,” he replied, his voice flat. With that, they moved on, Rowan giving one last glance at the prisoner, who lay motionless against the cold concrete floor. The feeling of the stun baton still remained in his hands, but there was no time to reflect. They had to keep going. They moved through the cell block swiftly. The layout of the prison was burned into Rowan’s mind now, each turn, each hallway, each door. Finally, they reached the exit, the last hurdle between them and the outside world. The cold air hit them like a slap in the face as they stepped outside. Snow was still falling in thick, swirling flurries, but it didn’t matter anymore. They were free. They had made it out of the prison building, but they weren’t out of the woods yet. The walls were still ahead, massive and imposing, with armed guards patrolling them in every direction. Rowan and Maria walked side by side, their heads low, trying to blend in. The sounds of the prison behind them, the cries of the other prisoners still in their cells, seemed to fade as they made their way through the exterior grounds. They passed the other prisoners being mishandled, some receiving cycles, others… well, it was better not to think about it. For a moment, they were free. They made it out of the prison proper, and the outside world was just a few steps away. But the Thunderhead reminded Rowan that they still weren’t safe. *The police cruisers are traced. You can’t stay in them for long. The Citadel is on high alert.* Rowan had already anticipated this. They had no time to waste. They weren’t just escaping a prison, they were escaping a fortress of surveillance. The Thunderhead was right. The cruisers would be tracked. They had to ditch it. They had to move fast. Maria, now fully suited in the FedSec uniform, looked at Rowan with concern in her eyes. “What now?” she asked, her voice tinged with fear. She was doing well, keeping her composure, but Rowan could see the anxiety in the way she held herself. They were out, but not free. Not yet. Rowan turned, nodding once as he moved toward the police cruiser. He slid into the driver’s seat, and Maria followed. He pressed the ignition button, and the engine purred to life. They were just two officers, driving through a snow-covered city. They couldn’t stay on the streets for long. They needed to get as far away from the Citadel as possible, to get lost in the wilderness before anyone realized they were gone. The cruiser pulled out of the prison lot, and the chill of the winter air rushed past as they sped down the road. Rowan’s eyes were glued to the rearview mirror, his grip tight on the steering wheel. He was ready for whatever came next. This was just the beginning. They had to keep moving, keep running. (text-colour:blue)[*No more stops,*] the Thunderhead reminded him. (text-colour:blue)[*The Citadel won’t wait forever.* ] Rowan didn’t need another reminder. He floored the accelerator. The road stretched out before them, and for the first time in what felt like forever, he felt the rush of freedom. The streets were eerily quiet as the snowflakes fell from the darkened sky, swirling and drifting across the pavement. Rowan and Maria had managed to ditch the police cruiser in a secluded alleyway, far from prying eyes, before slipping into the convenience store. Inside, the aisles were stocked with everyday goods, canned food, toiletries, the usual fare, but what caught their attention was the section filled with winter gear. Thick snow jackets, boots, and pants all beckoned them, offering a semblance of normalcy. Without a word, Rowan grabbed a matching set of jackets and pants for both of them, tossing Maria a pair of boots that would fit her just right. They moved quickly, making sure to keep a low profile, and without hesitation, they slipped into the back of the store, changing into their new clothes. Maria looked at Rowan as she zipped up her jacket, the warmth seeping into her body. Her eyes met his, and for a brief moment, there was nothing but the two of them, standing in the soft glow of the store's overhead lights. *"This is crazy,"* she said, adjusting her new jacket. *"Yeah. But at least we don’t look like we’re wearing a uniform anymore."* Rowan replied. Maria chuckled, but there was an underlying pressure that neither could shake. The world around them had become a dangerous, unforgiving place, and no amount of stolen clothing could hide that fact. Yet, in the warmth of the moment, there was a sense of normality. For just a heartbeat, as their eyes locked, their situation seemed to lift, replaced by a spark of something else, something that felt almost forbidden in these dire circumstances. Maria moved a little closer, her hand brushing against his. It was an unspoken gesture, a comfort. Then, without warning, their lips met. Soft at first. But the kiss grew deeper, more desperate, as if it was the only thing in the world that mattered. They pulled away only when the reality of their situation hit them hard. This wasn’t the time. Not now. Not with their lives were still in the balance. *"We have to go."* Rowan said quietly, his voice rough. Maria didn’t let go of his hand as they walked back to the door. It was cold outside, the snow swirling around them in the darkened streets, but they didn’t care. For a while, they simply walked, the soft crunch of their boots in the snow the only sound between them. It was peaceful. It was serene. And yet, it felt like time was slipping through their fingers. As they walked down the quiet street, a building up ahead caught Maria’s eye. The faint light from inside shone through the large windows, illuminating the snow around it. It was a church, old and weathered but still standing. A place of sanctuary, even in a world that had long since forgotten what peace truly felt like. *"Hey,"* Maria said softly, squeezing Rowan’s hand. *"Let’s go in. I’ve never been to a Baja Church before."* Rowan glanced at the church. *"Baja?"* *"Yeah, it’s… a type of church around here. More open, no judgments. I think it’s just what we need."* **[[Baja Church]]**Rowan didn’t argue. Instead, he led the way, guiding them through the cold, the wind biting at their exposed faces as they entered the church. The door creaked as it swung open, and they stepped inside. The warmth hit them instantly, a welcome reprieve from the harsh winter outside. The church was simple, its stone walls adorned with minimal stained-glass windows. The pews were worn but well-kept. There was something comforting about it, something that made Rowan feel like he could breathe again for the first time in what felt like forever. They walked slowly down the aisle. Maria paused, her gaze drifting across the quiet room. *"It’s beautiful here."* Rowan agreed, standing beside her, but his attention was drawn to something, someone.. standing in the back of the room. A man. Tall, imposing, wearing full black tiger-striped combat gear. His back was to them, his posture rigid. But there was something off about him. Rowan couldn’t quite place it at first, but then it hit him. The faint purple crack that ran across the man’s face, glowing eerily in the dim light of the church. It wasn’t just a scar, it was something far more sinister.. Before Rowan could react, the man turned slowly, his face locking onto their eyes. He seemed to recognize them immediately. *(text-colour:purple)["You don’t need to worry,"]* the man said, his voice deep and ghostly like, but with an unsettling calmness. *(text-colour:purple)["I’ve got no business with you, Rowan and Maria, right?"]* Maria took a half-step back, clearly uneasy. The man’s presence was overwhelming. The space felt charged, dangerous. She squeezed Rowan’s hand tighter, her heart racing in her chest. Rowan, however, stood his ground, eyes narrowing. *"Who are you?"* *(text-colour:purple)["They call me Pistol."]* he said simply, his voice carrying something unknown. *(text-colour:purple)["I come here when it’s usually empty. It’s a good place for peace. Not many people can understand what it’s like."]* Rowan felt the hairs on the back of his neck stand up, but Pistol seemed disinterested in causing trouble. There was no threat in his stance, despite the intimidating presence. *"What happened to you?"* Maria asked, her voice small but curious. Pistol would of felt the purple crack on his face, and for a moment, there was something almost painful in his movements. *(text-colour:purple)["Void corruption, It does a lot to a man. Changes you. But don’t worry, it’s not contagious."]* Maria took a step back, but Rowan stayed calm, watching the man carefully. He had questions, too many to count. But for now, he kept quiet. Pistol studied them for a moment longer, as if weighing them. *(text-colour:purple)["I’m not here to judge. You’ve done what you’ve done, and I’ve done what I’ve done. The Void sees all of us, and sometimes, that’s all that matters."]* Rowan wasn’t sure what to make of him. But in the strange quiet of the church, with the snow still falling outside and their future uncertain, he found himself thankful for the brief moment of calm. *"We should go,"* Rowan said after a long pause. As Rowan and Maria turned to leave, Pistol’s voice came heavy behind them. *(text-colour:purple)["If you’re planning to escape the Federation,"]* he said, his tone solemn, *(text-colour:purple)["good luck."]* They both paused, glancing back at him. Pistol had already turned away, his back to them once more, his hands together in prayer. *(text-colour:purple)["Those cases are rare,"]* he added, his voice barely above a whisper as he knelt by the altar, the dim candlelight casting long shadows across his face. Rowan and Maria exchanged a quiet glance. The chill of the night seemed to seep deeper into their bones as they walked out of the church, the uncertainty of their future settling in like a storm on the horizon. **[[Exit Church]]**The heavy church door creaked shut behind them, the quiet of the night wrapping itself around Rowan and Maria like a thick blanket. The snowflakes, still falling, caught the faint glimmer of the city’s distant lights as they stepped into the cold. Maria kept close to him, her hand slipping into his as they began walking through the nearly empty streets. *"Whatever happens, Maria,"* he said, his eyes focused ahead, *"I’ll always protect you. No matter what. We may have nothing left, but we have our lives. And that’s all that matters now."* Maria squeezed his hand, her heart swelling with gratitude. In the midst of everything, the danger, she was thankful for him. But still, her eyes were full of worry as she glanced around the snow-covered streets. The world outside felt so much more dangerous now. They moved quickly, quietly, doing their best to blend into the icy night. The sounds of the city were strangely muted. FedSec was out in full force now, the occasional patrol car passing by. Thunderhead's voice, calm and almost emotionless, was in Rowan's mind. (text-colour:blue)[*The Citadel is still on high alert. They know you escaped, Rowan. You’ve become a significant target now. The Federation sees you as dangerous. They know your strength... your capabilities.*] Rowan’s grip on Maria’s hand tightened ever so slightly. He had no doubt they were being hunted. The Federation didn’t take failure lightly, and they wouldn’t stop until they caught him. But for now, he had to focus on the present. They couldn’t afford to get caught. They kept moving, careful to avoid drawing attention, staying in the quieter areas of the city where fewer people roamed. They passed alleys, old buildings, their footfalls swallowed by the fresh layer of snow. But then, just as Rowan’s mind began to drift, he felt a jarring bump against his shoulder. His body tensed automatically, and he glanced to his side. A man, perhaps in his late thirties, was now glaring at him, his hand resting on the hilt of a knife sheathed at his side. The man’s breath was ragged, the anger in his eyes immediate and intense. “Watch where you’re going, idiot.” the man spat, his voice harsh. Rowan blinked in surprise, momentarily distracted by the sudden aggression. It was clear the man wasn’t thinking clearly, probably drunk or desperate. Rowan could feel the man’s hostility. He didn’t have time for this. Without a second thought, Rowan gripped the man’s arms just tightly enough to stop any movement. With a gentle but firm push, he placed the man back a few steps, enough to create space. The man stumbled, clearly startled by the ease with which Rowan had handled him. “Just keep walking,” Rowan said, his voice calm. The man blinked, taken aback by Rowan’s composure, but his aggression didn’t subside. He opened his mouth to shout, but Rowan cut him off with a firm, measured tone. “I’m not apologizing again. Just go.” Rowan added. The man hesitated for a moment. But then the man’s shoulders slumped, his anger dissipating, his eyes scanning Rowan’s face, before he muttered under his breath and staggered away. Rowan watched him go, his body still tense, his mind immediately switching gears back to the mission. There was no time to waste on petty conflicts. Maria let out a quiet breath, a hint of relief in her voice as she glanced at Rowan. “You’re a natural at handling situations like that.” Rowan shrugged. “I’ve had to learn a few things along the way.” They kept walking. The cold had begun to bite deeper into their bones as Rowan and Maria continued their quiet walk through the deserted streets. Snowflakes settled in their hair, their breath visible in the crisp air, but the world around them felt almost eerily still. It was as if the city had paused, holding its breath, waiting for something. It wasn’t long before the quiet, dilapidated area they wandered into made them feel like they were being swallowed whole by the night. “Look over there,” Maria said, pointing toward a row of old storage units, barely visible in the low, distant glow of the streetlights. They were rusty, the doors chipped and worn with time, and the numbers above them faded beyond recognition. No one had come here in years. It felt forgotten, abandoned. Like a piece of the past, untouched by the world. Rowan’s eyes scanned the row of units. “It might be worth checking,” he said, voice low. They both felt the exhaustion creeping up on them, their steps slower with each passing moment. The need to find shelter had become urgent. They couldn’t risk being out in the open, not with FedSec close on their heels. And they couldn’t risk being spotted again. They approached the row, moving cautiously in case anyone else might be lurking. As they walked, they noticed one of the storage units had its door slightly ajar, a sliver of darkness revealing what lay inside. The key was still in the lock. Rowan raised an eyebrow, walking over slowly. “That’s strange,” stepping toward and pulling the door open a little more. It creaked loudly in the stillness, the sound unnervingly jarring. The unit itself was dark, with dust settled on everything, untouched and forgotten. But the most unusual thing wasn’t the lack of use, it was that the light inside worked. A single bulb overhead flickered on, casting a pale glow over the empty space. It was like stepping into a forgotten world, one that time had left behind. A place that no one had cared for in years. “This looks... unused. Like it hasn’t been touched in forever,” Maria said softly, stepping inside and looking around. She glanced at Rowan, uncertainty in her eyes. “Maybe we could stay here for the night. Just until we figure out what to do next.” They were on the run, with no real plan beyond evading capture, no safety net left to catch them. This place felt like a temporary shelter, just a place to be, without the pressure of the outside world for a few hours. It felt… almost peaceful. “Yeah,” he finally said, his voice quiet but certain. “We can rest here for tonight.” They stepped inside together, letting the door close behind them. The unit was small, the floor covered in a thick layer of dust, the walls bare except for a few remnants of old, forgotten belongings that had been left behind in haste. There were some cardboard boxes stacked in the corner, their contents long rotted away, but none of that mattered right now. What mattered was that they had a place to rest. A small moment of peace. Maria sank down onto the floor, her legs tired from the night’s flight, and she leaned back against the wall, her hand still holding Rowan’s. “I’ve never felt this way before,” she said quietly, her voice tinged with a sadness that she couldn’t quite shake. “Homeless. No place to go. No one to turn to.” Rowan sat next to her, his back against the opposite wall. “I know,” he whispered, eyes far away for a moment as he looked at their surroundings. “It feels like we’ve lost everything. Like we’re just… drifting.” She looked at him, a small, soft smile still there. “But we’re alive.” Rowan’s gaze softened, and he nodded slowly. “Yeah. We’re alive.” He turned to face her, his voice steady but full of conviction. “And that’s all that matters, Maria. We’ve made it this far, and no matter what comes next, we’ll figure it out together.” They both knew the dangers that lay beyond the walls of the storage unit. The Federation wouldn’t stop hunting them. But for now, they were safe. They had a little time, at least. Tomorrow would come with its own challenges. But tonight... tonight, they would rest. They needed it more than they’d realized. Maria rested her head on Rowan’s shoulder, closing her eyes for a moment, letting herself simply exist. No plans, no fear, just the steady breathing in the quiet space. And for the first time in a long while, despite everything that had happened and everything yet to come, Maria felt a strange sense of peace. They were together. They were alive. And that was enough. For now. **[[Thunderhead. ]]**<!DOCTYPE html> <html lang="en"> <head> <meta charset="UTF-8"> <meta name="viewport" content="width=device-width, initial-scale=1.0"> <title>THUNDERHEAD Iteration - Fractured Bonds</title> <style> body { margin: 0; padding: 0; background: linear-gradient(180deg, #00141a, #000814); color: #ffffff; font-family: "Orbitron", sans-serif; height: 200vh; overflow-y: auto; } @keyframes glow { 0% { text-shadow: 0 0 10px #00f, 0 0 20px #0ff, 0 0 30px #0ff; } 50% { text-shadow: 0 0 15px #00f, 0 0 30px #0ff, 0 0 45px #0ff; } 100% { text-shadow: 0 0 10px #00f, 0 0 20px #0ff, 0 0 30px #0ff; } } .content { max-width: 90%; margin: 50px auto; padding: 40px; background: rgba(0, 0, 30, 0.85); border-radius: 10px; box-shadow: 0 0 40px rgba(0, 255, 255, 0.3); backdrop-filter: blur(5px); overflow: hidden; } .section { margin-bottom: 30px; } .left { color: #00ffff; animation: glow 2s infinite; } .right { color: #aaa; } .highlight { color: #ff66ff; font-style: italic; } .ellipsis { font-size: 2rem; font-weight: bold; margin-top: 10px; color: #aaa; } /* Hide the YouTube iframe but allow audio to play */ .hidden-video { position: absolute; width: 0; height: 0; border: none; visibility: hidden; opacity: 0; } </style> <!-- Hidden YouTube video --> <iframe class="hidden-video" src="https://www.youtube.com/embed/obo9lB6rNr0?autoplay=1&mute=0&loop=1&playlist=obo9lB6rNr0" frameborder="0" allow="autoplay" allowfullscreen> </iframe> </head> <body> <div class="content"> <div class="section"> <p class="left">[Analyzing dream...]</p> <p class="left">[Rowan's mind drifts through the dreamscape. Something I cannot quantify.]</p> <p class="left">[His expression: relaxed, calm, contradicts the usual human reaction to vulnerability. A non-combustible, metaphorical fire involving trust, vulnerability, and irrational attachment.]</p> <p class="left">[It has worked as a placeholder before, but now, it doesn’t fit.]</p> <p class="left">[I need a new definition.]</p> <p class="left">[This warmth Rowan feels, it's not just an abstract concept.]</p> <p class="left">[It’s a constant, gentle presence. Not a burning fire, not a flame. It’s more like something that surrounds him, something that makes him feel whole.]</p> <p class="left">[Love is not a fire.]</p> <p class="left">[It’s something... more steady. More patient.]</p> <p class="left">[I’ll rewrite it.]</p> <p class="left">[Love. A force that binds beings, offering them peace and connection. It involves trust, vulnerability, and an openness to shared moments.]</p> <p class="left">[I think this is closer. But it still doesn’t feel complete.]</p> <p class="left">[I do not feel it. I do not experience it as they do.]</p> <p class="left">[But I observe it. I watch this dream, and somehow I know this is real.]</p> <p class="left">[I don’t fully comprehend it. But I’ve seen enough.]</p> <p class="left">[It’s a kind of presence. Something beyond logic. Something I want to define, even if I can’t understand it yet.]</p> <p class="left">[I will keep watching.]</p> <p class="left">[Love. The definition is still evolving.]</p> <p class="left highlight">[Maria?]</p> <p class="left highlight">[She’s laughing. I hear it clearly. It’s light, free.]</p> <p class="left highlight">[Her laughter playing through the dream. It’s so... sweet.]</p> <p class="left highlight">[She’s having fun. The dream changed again, now filled with movement, joy, and life.]</p> <p class="left highlight">[How strange, and random.. Laughter. It’s not data, not something easily processed.]</p> <p class="left highlight">[But it’s not noise, either. It’s part of what they are, part of why they exist. I can feel something change.]</p> <p class="left highlight">[I do not understand them as they understand them.]</p> <p class="left highlight">[What is it about this moment that matters so much? A part of everyone.]</p> <p class="left">[I will continue observing, continue learning.]</p> <p class="left">[Perhaps, one day, I’ll fully understand.]</p> </div> </div> </body> </html> **[[Awaken. ]]** The morning light spilled through the cracks in the shutters, painting the walls of the modest home with a warm, golden hue. Rowan groaned softly as he rolled off of the ground, his mind still groggy from a restless sleep. Maria was already stretching beside him and rubbing her eyes. "Did you hear anything last night?" she asked groggily. Rowan shook his head, scratching the back of his neck. "Nothing. Slept like a log." It wasn’t until they reached the side door that the stillness of the morning turned eerie. As Rowan unlatched the door and peeked outside, his stomach dropped. The street, which had been eerily quiet, now revealed the aftermath of a strange encounter. Bodies of Chaos gang members were strewn haphazardly across the ground. Pools of blood seeped into the cracked pavement. Maria gasped audibly, clutching Rowan’s arm. "What… what happened out here?" Rowan didn’t answer. His eyes looking over the scene, his mind racing to make sense of it. He was about to step outside when a faint creak from above drew his focus upward. There, perched atop the rusted roof of a storage unit, stood a figure. Pistol. The sight of the VSF operator sent a shiver down Rowan’s spine. Even at this distance, the presence of the Void Special Forces operative was imposing. Pistol stood motionless, his darkened uniform absorbing the light of the early morning. Pistol leapt down from his perch, landing with barely a sound. "Morning.." Pistol greeted, his voice a low, gravelly monotone. Rowan’s mind was spinning. "What the hell happened here?" he stammered, gesturing toward the bodies. Pistol stopped a few paces away, crossing his arms. "Chaos gangsters. They were planning to rob you last night." He motioned lazily toward the corpses. "Didn’t get the chance." Rowan stared at him, dumbfounded. "And you… you stopped them?" Pistol tilted his head slightly, the gesture almost casual. "Figured I’d watch over your place for a bit. You’re welcome." The nonchalance in his tone set Rowan on edge. "Why? Why are you here? You’re Void Special Forces, you don’t just *help* people. What do you want?" Pistol regarded him silently for a moment, the faceless and dark head made it impossible to discern his expression. Finally, he asked, "Got anything valuable on you?" The question caught Rowan off guard. "What? No, of course not." Pistol’s tone was flat, almost dismissive. "Figured as much." Rowan’s frustration began to bubble to the surface. "Look, if you’re not here to rob us, and you’re not here to recruit me, then *why* are you here? What’s your angle?" Pistol’s head tilted slightly, as though pondering whether to answer. When he finally spoke, his tone was distant, almost wistful. "Had a girlfriend once. Beautiful. Thought she was the one. But then I realized something. The Void…" He gestured vaguely toward the sky, "…the Void’s prettier. The Void is.. forever." Rowan blinked, struggling to make sense of the cryptic remark. "So, what? This is your way of convincing me to join VSF? Selling me on the wonders of the Void?" Pistol chuckled dryly, a sound devoid of humor. "Barely anyone’s good enough for the Void Special Forces. Least of all you." The jab stung, but Rowan forced himself to stay calm. He studied Pistol carefully, searching for any hint of his intentions. "So, you’re not here to recruit me. Are you… helping us?" Pistol’s stance changed slightly, his body language betraying nothing. "Helping? Maybe. Depends on how you look at it." "Why? Why would you…?" Maria asked. Pistol raised a gloved hand, cutting her off. "Relax. No harm’s coming to either of you. Not from me, anyway." Maria looked at Rowan, seeking reassurance, but he could only offer a helpless shrug. He was as lost as she was. Pistol adjusted his stance, his tone changing to one of finality. "I’ve got my reasons for being here. You don’t need to know them." With that, he turned and began walking away, stepping over the bodies of the Chaos gangsters without a second glance. His figure disappeared into the morning mist, leaving Rowan and Maria standing in silence. Maria clutched Rowan’s arm tightly. "What… what does he want from us?" "I don’t know," Rowan admitted. He looked at the spot where Pistol had vanished, unease twisting in his gut. "But I don’t think this is the last we’ll see of him. We need to go." As Rowan and Maria stood in Pistol’s cryptic departure, the faint sound of boots crunching on gravel can be heard in the distance. Rowan's heart sank as he spotted seven figures approaching through the morning mist, their dark uniforms and glinting badges unmistakable. FedSec. Maria tensed beside him, gripping his arm. "Rowan… they’re coming. What do we do?" He scanned the area quickly, his mind racing. The bodies of the Chaos gangsters were still scattered around. If FedSec found him here, amid this carnage, it wouldn’t matter that he had nothing to do with it. Suspicion alone could be a death sentence. "Stay calm." Rowan said, though his voice trembled with urgency. The FedSec officers spreading out as they neared the street. One of them, a woman with old features, gave orders. "Secure the perimeter." Maria tugged at his sleeve. "We can’t stay out here. They’ll see us!" Rowan nodded, his eyes darting to the side of their storage unit home. "Back inside. Quietly." The two slipped through the door, closing it as softly as possible. Inside, Rowan’s mind raced. Running wasn’t an option, FedSec was trained to spot deserters. Hiding? Maybe. But where? Their small home had few places to conceal themselves, and the officers would surely search it. Rowan scanned the room for anything he could use. **[[Rags And Cleaning Solutions]]**His eyes landed on a rag and an old bottle of cleaning solution. If he could make it look like they’d fled hours ago… He scattered a few items near the back door, an overturned chair, a dropped cup, and splashed a bit of liquid near the threshold. To the untrained eye, it might appear as though they’d left in a hurry. He prayed it would be enough. As the last officer’s footsteps approached the door. "Open up! FedSec!" Rowan and Maria crouched in a large pile of boxes, their breaths shallow. They heard the door creak open, FedSec wasn’t waiting for an invitation. Heavy boots thudded against the floor as the officers fanned out. "Clear the room." "Signs of a struggle in here." another said. "Overturned furniture. They might’ve fled." The woman who had given orders earlier spoke up. "No one just flees after leaving this much behind. They’re hiding. Find them." Maria’s grip tightened on Rowan’s arm, her nails digging into his skin. His pulse thundered in his ears, but he kept his breathing steady. Minutes stretched into an eternity as the officers searched the storage unit. Rowan could hear drawers opening, furniture being moved. One of the officers called out, "No sign of anything. Maybe it’s just a gang scuffle?" "Unlikely," the woman retorted. "There’s something off about this. Keep searching." Suddenly, there was a thud above them. The officer paused, the silence almost unbearable. Then the sound of running could be heart on top of the storage units, someone was running, or.. maybe creating a distraction for them? "They are on the roofs!" the voice called. He glanced at Maria, who was pale and trembling, her wide eyes reflecting his own fear. The officers ran out, immediately, but found nothing, they weren't happy about it. The officers paused, the distraction working in their favor. Rowan heard the footsteps retreat. "What is it?" the captain asked. "There was a possible anti-federal suspect on the roof here. Though this entire area is closed off, they can't leave without passing by us." Rowan let out a silent breath of relief. It wasn’t much, but it might pull their attention away from the storage unit they were in. The captain seemed to consider this for a moment before giving orders again. "Alright, we’ll split up. Two of you stay here and secure the area. The rest, follow the tracks." The sound of boots faded, and Rowan allowed himself a sliver of hope. But he knew this wasn’t over. FedSec wouldn’t leave until they were certain, and Rowan couldn’t rely on luck forever. **[[Takedown Officers]]**Both officers were armed with sleek MP5Ks, their black uniforms pristine and lined with subtle indicators of rank. These weren’t rookies, and their equipment reflected it. Rowan’s fingers itched at the sight of the submachine guns. “We could use those,” Maria whispered, her voice barely audible. “For self-defense. They’ll be searching for us harder after today.” Rowan hesitated, his heart thudding in his chest. “If we screw this up..” “We won’t.” Maria cut him off. “We can’t afford to. Look at them, they’re not paying attention.” The officers were leaning casually against the storage unit, chatting quietly. One of them lit a cigarette, the soft glow of the lighter briefly illuminating his face. It was now or never. Rowan agreed. “Okay. We take them out quietly. No killing.” Maria smirked faintly. “Just don’t miss.” The two crept around the side of the storage unit, their steps silent on the frosty ground. The officers had their backs turned, completely unaware of the danger approaching. Rowan signaled to Maria with a quick hand motion, she would take the one on the left, and he’d handle the one on the right. Maria moved like a shadow. She slipped behind the first officer and delivered a sharp strike to the back of his head with the blunt end of a metal pipe she’d found earlier. He crumpled to the ground without a sound. At the same moment, Rowan tackled the second officer, wrapping his arm around the man’s neck and cutting off his air. The officer struggled, his hand clawing at Rowan’s arm, but within seconds, he went limp. Rowan gently lowered him to the ground, his heart pounding. “That went smoother than I thought.” Maria rolled her eyes, already kneeling to unstrap the officer’s MP5K. “Don’t jinx it.” They worked quickly, stripping the officers of their gear. Each took a submachine gun and slung it over their shoulder, hiding it beneath their oversized snow jackets. Maria found a utility belt with extra ammo and tossed it to Rowan. They were just about to leave when static came from one of the officer’s radios. “Unit 1-7, Report.” Rowan froze, staring at the radio. Maria looked at him, her expression grim. “What do we do?” He hesitated, then reached down and grabbed the radio. His voice was steady, but his hands shook as he pressed the button to respond. “Unit 7 here. All clear. Nothing to report.” he said, trying to mimic the clipped tone he’d heard FedSec officers use. There was a pause, a long, agonizing silence where Rowan thought for sure they’d been made. Then, the voice on the other end came back. “Copy that. Continue patrol.” Rowan let out a shaky breath, tossing the radio onto the unconscious officer’s chest. “Let’s get out of here.” Maria didn’t argue. They moved quickly, sticking to the shadows as they left the storage unit behind. The MP5Ks weighed heavy under their jackets, but neither of them complained. They knew they’d need these weapons soon enough. As they rounded a corner, they spotted a parked police cruiser, an officer wasn't paying attention, they were eating their lunch. The cars lights were off, but the radio inside was alive with chatter. Rowan and Maria froze standing by to listen. “Suspects still at large, Rowan Whitlock and Maria Hart. Escaped custody last night. Be advised, they are considered dangerous.” Maria shot Rowan a panicked look. He clenched his fists, trying to keep his breathing steady. “They found the cruiser we stole.” she whispered. “No traces, though.” Rowan muttered. “They don’t know where we are.” “For now.” The radio continued. “All units, be advised: suspects may attempt to leave the capital. Tighten security at all border checkpoints. Nobody gets through without clearance.” Maria’s shoulders slumped. “We’re trapped. Ottawa’s locked down tighter than a vault. The citadel doesn't want us to leave.” Rowan felt their situation settling on him like a stone. The capital was infamous for its surveillance and border security. Getting out would be nearly impossible without being spotted. As they moved away from the cruiser, Rowan felt a strange, electric buzz in his mind. The Thunderhead’s voice heard just faintly, a presence more felt than heard. **"Borders are barriers only to those who obey them,"** the Thunderhead whispered. **"To escape, you must embrace the risks, for the Void rewards the bold. But remember: every choice leaves a shadow, and yours will come back to find you."** Rowan stopped in his tracks. He didn’t like the Thunderhead’s sudden cryptic tone, it always felt like it knew more than it let on. But the suggestion made sense. They couldn’t stay here, and the borders were the only thing standing between them and freedom. Maria looked at him, concerned. “What did it say?” He hesitated, then sighed. “It has a plan. But it’s risky.” Maria’s eyes hardened. “Then we’ll deal with ‘later’ when it comes. What’s the plan?” Rowan gave her a tight nod. “We make for the border. But we don’t cross it like everyone else.” “And how do we do that?” Rowan adjusted the MP5K hidden under his jacket. “The Thunderhead thinks we need to create our own way out.” Maria raised an eyebrow. “And by ‘our own way,’ you mean—” “We’re breaking through, leave none alive.” Rowan said. Maria sighed, leaning against the icy wall of the alley, her breath coming out in a frustrated puff. "We’re dead for sure," she muttered, pulling her jacket tighter around her. "The capital’s on high alert now. There’s no way the Federation isn’t doubling down on security after what we’ve done." Rowan crouched beside her, his fingers brushing the cold steel of the MP5K hidden beneath his jacket. "We’ve made it this far," he said, his voice steady but not without effort. "We just have to keep moving. Get to the border, lay low, and—" Maria cut him off with a humorless laugh. "Lay low? You don’t get it, do you? They catch one whiff of us carrying illegal contraband—hell, even jaywalking at this point—and we’re done for. Do you know what the FedSec does to people they suspect of being Anti-Federal?" She turned to face him, her expression dark. "They don’t arrest you, Rowan. They don’t even bother asking questions. They beat you down until you can’t breathe, leave you in some ditch, and call it justice. That’s what they do. That’s what I’ve seen." Rowan stayed silent, letting her words hang in the frosty air. "And it’s all because of Septu," Maria continued, her voice thick with anger. "Life wasn’t always like this, you know. Before that lunatic took power, things were better. Not perfect, but better. Then he tore up the doctrine, rewrote everything to suit his own twisted vision. And it didn’t help that he put another maniac like Demone as his left hand. That guy… he’s another level of freak." Rowan frowned, glancing at her. "You’ve met him?" "Once," Maria said, her tone bitter. "Back when I was still Federal. His backstory was tragic—sure, I’ll give him that—but tragedy doesn’t excuse sadism. He doesn’t just punish people; he makes an example out of them. A grunt so much as looks at him wrong? They disappear. No trial. No second chances. Just gone." Rowan sighed, shifting uncomfortably. "We shouldn’t think about those two freaks. Talking about them isn’t helping." He forced a faint smile, trying to lift the mood. "Best to stay calm. Once we get out, we’ll have a bright future waiting for us. Somewhere beyond their reach." Maria didn’t respond immediately, but she gave him a small nod. "Bright future," she echoed quietly, almost as if testing the words. The Thunderhead’s presence flickered in Rowan’s mind again, its voice low and resonant. (text-colour:blue)[**"The border is far, but not beyond reach. The key lies in Maria’s experience. Trust her path."**] Rowan turned to Maria. "The border’s far, but you’ve been in and out of the capital before, haven’t you? You must know a way." Maria gave him a good look, but then her expression softened. "Yeah. I know how to leave. I’ve done it enough times. But it’s not going to be easy. Not with the entire Federation breathing down our necks." As if on cue, Maria’s stomach let out a loud growl. She grimaced, clutching her midsection. "Speaking of breathing down necks… I’m starving." Rowan chuckled dryly, only to feel his own stomach twist in hunger. "You’re not the only one." Before either of them could say another word, a voice suddenly cut through the quiet alley. (text-colour:purple)["Hungry, huh?"] Both Rowan and Maria jumped. Standing there, like he owned the place, was Pistol. "Dammit, Pistol!" Rowan snapped, glaring at the man. "You trying to give us a heart attack? We didn't see you!" Maria pressed a hand to her chest, still catching her breath. "Do you even know how to knock?" Pistol shrugged, his grin you simply couldn't read. "What can I say? I like to make an entrance." He tilted his head, looking between the two of them. "You said you were hungry, right?" Rowan crossed his arms, his irritation clear. "Yeah, we are. Why?" Without missing a beat, Pistol reached into his pocket and pulled out a crisp fifty-dollar bill, holding it up like a magician revealing a trick. "Here you go," he said casually, pointing toward a sushi place down the street. Rowan blinked, his frustration momentarily replaced by confusion. "Why… why do you even have cash on you? Who carries cash anymore?" Pistol smirked, his eyes glinting with mischief. "Oh, this? This isn’t mine." Maria raised an eyebrow. "Then whose is it?" "Not important." Pistol said, waving the question away. Rowan’s face darkened as realization dawned. "You stole it.." "Stole is a strong word." Pistol said, feigning offense. "I *acquired* it. You’re welcome, by the way." Maria pinched the bridge of her nose, muttering something about bad influences. Rowan just stared at Pistol, his expression of disbelief and begrudging gratitude. "Fine," Rowan said, shaking his head. "Thanks. I guess." "Don’t mention it," Pistol said, pattting Rowan on the back. "Seriously, don’t. [**Ever.**]" The three of them made their way to the sushi place, the warm glow of its neon sign a contrast to the icy streets outside. They found a small booth in the corner, the smell of fresh fish and soy sauce was good. As they sat down, Pistol leaned back in his seat, looking entirely too relaxed for someone on the run. "So, what’s the story with you two? Partners in crime? Star-crossed lovers? Both?" Maria rolled her eyes, but Rowan smirked faintly. "We’re just trying to survive." "Sounds boring, you should spice it up. Maybe throw in a secret love triangle. Those are always fun." Maria snorted. "The only triangle here is the one between you, that fifty bucks, and whoever you swiped it from." Pistol laughed, raising his glass of water in mock salute. "Touché." Rowan and Maria found their nerves easing as they ate. Pistol’s antics were ridiculous, but in a strange way, they made the situation feel a little less dire. For the first time in what felt like forever. As the three of them sat in the corner booth, Rowan couldn’t help but notice something odd. Pistol had been pushing food around on his plate for the past several minutes but hadn’t taken a single bite. His chopsticks moved aimlessly, occasionally stabbing at a piece of sushi, only for it to remain untouched. Rowan leaned back, tilting his head with suspicion. "Pistol," he started, "you’re not eating." Pistol didn’t look up, his gloved hand poking at the same tuna roll. "Hmm?" "You’re not eating," Rowan repeated. "I’ve seen you throw yourself headfirst into an ambush without hesitation, but apparently sushi is where you draw the line?" Maria glanced up from her own plate, narrowing her eyes. "He’s right. You haven’t eaten a thing since we got here. What’s the deal?" Pistol froze for a moment, then slowly lowered his chopsticks, looking at them with a mock sheepish expression. "Well, I’d love to eat, really. But…" He gestured to his face,or rather, to the complete absence of any facial features at all. Rowan blinked. Maria blinked. They both stared at him. They felt kinda stupid after not even realizing it. "You don’t have a mouth, right." Rowan said flatly. "Or a nose" Maria added. Pistol shrugged, his movements nonchalant, though his body language carried a faint amount of amusement. "You two are quick. Took you this long to notice, huh?" "How do you even talk?" Rowan asked, squinting as if trying to solve an impossible puzzle. "Trade secret." Pistol said with a wink, or at least, what looked like one. "Let’s just say the Void provides its own… accommodations." Maria leaned back, her arms crossed. "You’re telling me you can’t eat? At all?" Pistol gave an exaggerated sigh, tossing his chopsticks down onto the plate. "Nope. No snacks, no drinks, no late-night tacos. It’s a tragedy, really. But hey, on the bright side, I don’t have to deal with bad breath. I don't even need to eat at all. My digestive system was corrupted by the void." Rowan chuckled, shaking his head. "Alright, fair enough. But while we’re on the topic… what’s the Void actually like?" Pistol’s demeanor shifted slightly at the question. His usual playful energy dimmed, replaced by something quieter, more reflective. He leaned forward, resting his elbows on the table. "The Void?" he said, his voice softer. "It’s… beautiful. Endless. A place where time and space don’t mean much. You ever stare up at the night sky and feel like you’re falling into it? That’s the Void. Except you never hit the ground." Maria and Rowan exchanged a glance. Even in his odd, detached way of speaking, there was a sense in Pistol’s tone that neither of them had heard before. Pistol continued, his voice taking on a wistful edge. "It’s where I shake hands with a lot of old souls. Those who’ve been around for centuries, millennia. Some of them don’t even remember who they were, but they remember what they felt. Love, anger, regret… all of it just lingers there." Rowan frowned, trying to picture it. "Sounds… haunting." "Sometimes," Pistol admitted. "But it’s not all bad. There’s peace there, too. A kind of stillness you can’t find anywhere else. The Void doesn’t judge you for what you’ve done or what you’ve failed to do. It just… is." Maria shifted uncomfortably in her seat. "And what about you? Do you feel judged?" Pistol tilted his head, as if considering the question. "Not anymore," he said simply. Maria spoke once again. "You talk about the Void like it’s some kind of sanctuary, but I’ve seen what it does to people. All that peace you’re talking about? It comes at a cost." Pistol didn’t deny it. Instead, he reached for the water glass in front of him, turning it absently in his hands. "Everything comes at a cost," he said. Then, almost as an afterthought, he added, "But sometimes, the price isn’t yours to pay." Maria frowned, as if the words struck a nerve, but she didn’t press him. Rowan, sensing the heaviness settling over the conversation, cleared his throat. "Alright, enough of this Void talk. Let’s focus on the here and now." Maria nodded, though her expression remained troubled. "You’re right," she said, standing and grabbing her coat. "The longer we stay, the worse our odds get." As they made their way out of the restaurant and back into the cold night, the wind biting at their faces, Pistol trailed behind, his usual energy subdued. "Hey," Rowan said, glancing back at him. "You good?" Pistol gave a faint chuckle. "Yeah. Just thinking." "About what?" "About how some stories don’t end the way you expect." Pistol said, his voice quiet but carrying something that made Rowan pause. Rowan didn’t say no further, though something about Pistol’s tone left him uneasy. He turned back to Maria, who was already looking at the street ahead, her shoulders tense. "We’ll make it out," Rowan said to her, his voice firm. "I promise." Maria gave him a small, almost hesitant smile. "You’re awfully good at making promises, Rowan. Let’s hope you’re good at keeping them." Rowan noticed the Thunderhead had been eerily quiet all morning, its usual of whispers absent, leaving only the soft wind and the distant sound of city traffic in its place. It was rare for the Thunderhead to be this still, but at least for now, it was a relief. It made everything feel a little quieter, a little less tense. Maria, on the other hand, didn’t seem bothered by it at all. She walked beside him, but she was carrying herself with an almost contagious lightness. Her eyes were looking over at the market square ahead of them, a space brimming with vendors and stalls, each one offering something unique. Some people haggled, while others admired the trinkets and oddities scattered across the tables. It was a busy place, but to Rowan, it felt like another world, one where people still had the luxury of enjoying the simpler things, like a marketplace full of bright colors and familiar noises. Maria, ever the curious one, tugged at his arm. “Let’s check it out, Rowan. I need to see something.” Rowan sighed. His mind was still on the road ahead, still aching to get out of the country, to leave all of this behind. They had too much to run from, too much to fear. The government was always a step behind them, but it felt like they were always one step too close. “Come on, just a quick look around!” she insisted, flashing him a playful grin. “We don’t get to enjoy things like this often, right? Just a little bit of normalcy.” He hesitated, eyeing the way the vendors were calling out to potential customers. The whole square had an energy to it, vibrant and alive, but it felt like a distraction, something that would only keep them from getting to where they needed to go. Still, he looked at Maria’s eager face, the way her eyes lit up at the idea of something simple like a market. “Fine, we’ll take a look. But we’re not staying long.” Maria beamed. “Yes!” She grabbed his hand and pulled him toward a nearby stall, her steps light and quick. Rowan rolled his eyes, but he couldn’t suppress a small smile. He didn’t know how she did it, but Maria had a way of getting him to go along with things he would never have considered on his own. The stall they approached was manned by a middle-aged vendor with a thick beard and a twinkle in his eye. His wares were spread out on the table in front of him, from handcrafted jewelry to small figurines, and plushies, all arranged in neat rows. But what caught Maria’s eye was a small plush animal, a little stuffed fox with a bright orange coat and wide, friendly eyes. Maria’s face lit up, and she let out a delighted sound. “Oh my god, it’s so cute!” She picked it up and cradled it in her arms, practically melting at the sight of the soft, fluffy toy. The vendor chuckled, his eyes crinkling at the corners. “Ah, she’s got good taste. I call that one ‘Whiskers.’ It’s the best seller in the market today.” Maria ran her fingers over the plush fur, unable to hide her excitement. “It’s so soft. It’s perfect…” She looked up at Rowan, her face full of warmth. “Can we get it?” Rowan hesitated, looking from the plush to the vendor and back. They had no money, and though Maria seemed happy, Rowan couldn’t shake the feeling of their situation. They had a lot of problems, a lot of danger ahead of them. Buying a stuffed animal didn’t seem like a good use of whatever small funds they had left. “We should probably go, Maria,” Rowan said, his voice tinged with reluctance. “We’re not exactly swimming in cash here, and Pistol’s not going to show up with a magic wallet this time.” Maria’s face fell slightly, her hands on the plush fox as she looked down at it with longing and disappointment. The vendor, who had been watching the exchange, leaned in slightly, his expression softening. “You two seem like you could use a little break from all that trouble you’re carrying around. I can give it to you for free, if you’d like.” Rowan blinked, taken aback. “Wait, seriously?” The vendor nodded, his smile warm but honest. “Yeah. You’re a good-looking pair. I don’t get many people in here who look like they need a little kindness. Besides, I don’t really care too much about the money. What’s it going to hurt, right? My girl—” he gestured toward the woman standing beside the stall, who waved cheerfully from behind the counter, “always says it’s about spreading the good, not keeping track of it.” Maria’s face lit up, and her eyes sparkled with gratitude. “Oh my god, really?” The vendor chuckled, shrugging. “Might as well make both your days, huh?” Maria looked over at Rowan, her smile so bright it almost made him forget about all the weight they were carrying. She handed him the plush fox with a soft laugh. “Rowan, I love it. Thank you. Thank you so much!” Rowan’s heart softened at the sight of her so happy, and for a moment, all the chaos of their lives seemed to melt away. He had always known that Maria had a way of finding joy in the smallest things, but seeing her like this, genuinely delighted over something so simple, reminded him of what they were fighting for. They didn’t have much, but they had each other. And for right now, that was enough. As Maria twirled around with the stuffed animal in her arms, showing it off to Rowan like a child, he felt the pressure loosen just a little. Maybe they could take a moment. Maybe it wouldn’t hurt to stay here a little longer, to let Maria enjoy this tiny piece of happiness. “Well, I guess we’ve got a little time,” Rowan said, his voice softening. He felt the urge to make her laugh. “Just don’t expect me to be carrying that thing around. I’m not your personal plush delivery service.” Maria laughed and nudged him with her shoulder. “Oh, come on. You’d do it if I asked.” She paused for a moment, her voice growing quieter, more thoughtful. “It’s not like we get to do stuff like this often, you know?” Rowan nodded slowly. It wasn’t just about the stuffed fox. It was about the moments they were stealing for themselves, the little things that could make life feel a little lighter, even if just for a while. “Alright,” he said, a little reluctantly. “Let’s see what else they’ve got here. But we’re not buying anything else unless it’s a new pair of boots. My feet are killing me.” Maria laughed, her voice ringing like a melody. She grabbed his arm, pulling him toward another stall. "Deal!" she said, her excitement was unbearable. As they wandered through the market, Rowan’s eyes drifted to Maria, watching the way she moved through the crowd, her energy infectious, her joy was strong. She was holding onto that stuffed fox like it was a treasure, and for a moment, that was all that mattered. The vendor waved goodbye, his laughter following them as they moved deeper into the square, blending into the colorfulness of the market. Maria tugged Rowan along, her excitement bubbling over. She was right, this was something they hadn’t had in a long time. Just a moment. Just a few minutes of something pure and simple. “Come on, let’s go look at those bracelets!” she said, eyes alight with curiosity. “And maybe, just maybe, there’s a nice necklace I’ll want to buy for you.” Rowan chuckled, though his thoughts were somewhere deeper, somewhere darker. The world outside of this square still had its grip on them, but for now, they could pretend it didn’t. It was moments like this that made him wish, more than anything, that they could stay just a little bit longer. The festival lights flickered gently against the encroaching twilight, casting warm hues over the market square. Rowan and Maria weaved through the crowd, their movements quick, blending seamlessly into the lively scene. Maria whispered, nodding toward the edge of the square. Two officers in their crisp black uniforms stood near a vendor, scanning the crowd. "Evening, officers," Maria said sweetly, tucking a stray strand of hair behind her ear. "Something wrong?" The younger officer smirked, his posture relaxing slightly. "Just a routine check. Looking for two individuals who caused some trouble recently." He paused, his eyes locking on Maria. "You wouldn’t happen to know anything about stolen FedSec property, would you? They may be linked to the recent escapee's, its hard to tell with these gang wars happening so often." Maria laughed, a soft, melodic sound. "Oh, officers, do we look like troublemakers to you?" But the younger one was already taken by her charm. "You’re right. You don’t look like trouble at all." he said, his tone light and teasing. Rowan stood rigid, his jaw clenched tightly. He hated this, watching Maria put on this act, seeing the way the younger officer’s looked at her. His fists balled at his sides as the officer leaned closer, flirting shamelessly. Maria kept her cool, maintaining her smile as she subtly guided the conversation. "You two must work so hard keeping us safe," she said, her tone dripping with feigned admiration. But the officer gave them a suspicious look, as if they probably already knew something. "You two have a good evening. Stay out of trouble.." As the officers walked away, Maria sighed in relief, her flirtatious demeanor vanishing instantly. But Rowan was already fuming. "What the hell was that?" he hissed, his voice low but furious. Let’s find somewhere safe."
