He tilted his head upward, as if addressing someone unseen. “Inanis,” he snarled. “Take hold.” Rowan blinked, confused. “Who the hell is Ina—” Before he could finish, the air around Septu seemed to rip apart, like heatwaves distorting the space. A faint, otherworldly glow began to emanate from his body, growing stronger with each passing second. Septu’s muscles tensed, and an invisible force seemed to pulse outward, cracking the asphalt beneath him. Rowan instinctively took a step back, his hands tightening around his weapon. “What… what is this?” Rowan muttered. Septu didn’t answer. He locked onto Rowan. He grinned, a terrifying, unhinged grin, as a voice only he could hear whispered in his mind. (text-colour:magenta)[*“Good. Let us break him.”*] Septu disappeared from sight. One moment, he was standing still, and the next, he was a blur of motion. Rowan barely had time to react before a deafening crash shattered the stillness. Septu’s fist slammed into the ground where Rowan had been standing a split second earlier, sending a shockwave ripping through the street. Chunks of asphalt exploded upward, pelting Rowan as he rolled to safety. “KEEP HER NAME OUT OF YOUR MOUTH!” Septu bellowed, his voice like thunder, filled with an anger that was almost feral. His eyes blazed with hatred, and for the first time, Rowan saw something else, pain. But there was no time to dwell on it. Rowan scrambled to his feet, breathing hard. He raised his weapon, firing a volley of rounds at Septu. The bullets struck true, but they didn’t even slow him down. The glowing energy surrounding Septu seemed to absorb the impact, the rounds disintegrating into harmless sparks. Septu lunged again, closing the distance between them with terrifying speed. Rowan ducked under a wild swing, the force of the blow brushing past him and cracking a nearby building’s facade. He countered with a strike from the butt of his MP5K, aiming for Septu’s temple. It connected, but Septu didn’t even flinch. Instead, he grabbed the SMG with one hand and flung it aside as if it were a toy. “You think you can hurt me?” Septu growled, his voice dripping with contempt. He swung again, this time with accuracy. Rowan barely managed to block with his arms, but the sheer force of the blow sent him skidding across the ground. His vision blurred as pain radiated through his body. Rowan coughed, tasting blood in his mouth. He struggled to his feet, glaring at Septu with defiance. “You’re nothing but a coward, hiding behind your power. You think that makes you strong?” Septu laughed, a chilling, hollow sound. “You don’t understand, do you?” He stepped forward, he was flaring with renewed intensity. “I *am* strength. And you? You’re just a fool who doesn’t know when to quit.” Rowan charged, a desperate low roar tearing from his throat. He aimed a punch at Septu’s jaw, putting everything he had into it. But Septu caught his fist effortlessly, his grip like iron. With a flick of his wrist, he twisted Rowan’s arm, forcing him to his knees. “Pathetic.” Septu hissed. He raised his other hand, now glowing with energy, and brought it down. Rowan barely managed to roll out of the way, the ground where he had been moments before exploding into a crater. He stumbled to his feet, swaying unsteadily. Septu was relentless. He pressed the attack, his strikes coming faster and harder, each one designed to break Rowan completely. Rowan fought back with everything he had, but it was clear he was outmatched. Septu’s movements were almost supernatural, guided by the unseen force of the Hollow King. Finally, Rowan collapsed to the ground, his body battered and broken. He tried to push himself up, but his arms gave out beneath him. Septu stood over him, his expression a mixture of triumph and disdain. “Do you see now?” Septu said, his voice low and dangerous. “This is what happens. This is what happens when you speak of her name.” Rowan looked up at him, his vision swimming. He didn’t have the strength to respond. All he could do was glare, his defiance still burning even as his body failed him. Septu raised his glowing hand one final time, the energy crackling ominously. But he hesitated. For a brief moment, his expression faltered, and Rowan thought he saw something—regret? Pain? It was gone in an instant, replaced by cold determination. “You’re not worth it,” Septu muttered, lowering his hand. He turned and walked away, his aura fading as the presence of the Hollow King receded. Rowan lay on the ground, his body broken but his spirit unyielding, as darkness claimed him. Through the haze of pain, Rowan’s eyes widened as he saw Septu pivot toward Maria. She stood firm, clutching her Fox Toy, “Stay back..” she screamed, her voice filled with defiance. Septu didn’t stop. His eyes was locked on her. Septu struck her in the back with his fist. The blow was devastating, sending her to her knees. She gasped in pain. “No!” Rowan shouted, struggling to rise. His muscles screamed in protest, but he managed to get onto his hands and knees. Before he could crawl forward, Demone's massive hand clamped down on his shoulder. The towering figure effortlessly dragged Rowan upright, holding him in place. Demone’s grip was like iron. “Watch.” Demone rumbled, his deep voice filled with dark amusement. Septu stood over Maria, a glowing energy blade forming in his hand. The purple light cast eerie shadows across his face. “Witness me.” he said, his voice low but commanding. Maria set the fox plushie gently on the ground, her fingers staying on it for a moment as she looked up at Septu, her eyes defiant even in the face of death. Septu didn’t hesitate. With a single thrust, he drove the energy blade straight through her chest. Time seemed to freeze. Maria’s eyes widened, her breath hitching before she collapsed onto the snow. The purple glow of the blade faded as Septu withdrew it, the snow beneath Maria darkening with blood. “Maria!” Rowan screamed, his voice was desperate, but no sound seemed loud enough to reach her. Demone released Rowan, letting him crumple to the ground. Septu turned, his hands sliding back into his pockets. He hopped effortlessly onto Demone’s shoulder. “ Let him remember what defiance costs,” Septu said coldly, his voice devoid of any remorse. Demone grinned, his jagged teeth glinting. “See you around, lovebird,” he said with a sickening chuckle as he carried Septu away, the two fading into the snowstorm. Rowan crawled toward Maria, his body shaking with sobs. He picked up the fox plushie, clutching it tightly as he cradled Maria’s lifeless form. Tears streamed down his face, falling onto the crimson-stained snow. The world around him blurred, his grief consuming everything. Her body was still warm, her chest rising and falling in shallow, uneven breaths. Her lips quivered as she tried to speak, her eyes half-open, struggling to focus on him. “Maria... stay with me,” Rowan begged. He cradled her in his arms, the fox plushie pressed against her chest, blood soaking into the fabric. His tears fell freely, mingling with the snow beneath them. “I’m here. I’ll get us out of this. I swear.” Maria’s hand weakly rose, brushing against Rowan’s cheek. Her touch was feeble but deliberate, grounding him even as his world shattered around him. She opened her mouth to speak, her voice barely audible, more a whisper carried by the wind. “Rowan… you… you fought so hard. Don’t… blame yourself.” “Don’t say that,” Rowan choked, shaking his head vehemently. “You’re going to be okay. We’ll get through this together. Just hold on.” A faint smile formed on her face, though it was laced with pain. “I… love you,” she said, her voice fading with each word. Her hand fell limp against his chest, her body relaxing in his arms. Rowan froze, staring at her, waiting, praying, for another breath, a sign of life. But none came. “No... no, no, no!” Rowan screamed, pulling her closer as if his embrace alone could bring her back. The snow fell heavier now, blanketing them in its cold, uncaring silence. His cries echoed into the void, but there was no answer, no comfort. He looked up, his vision blurred by tears, and screamed into the storm. “Thunderhead! Where were you? Why didn’t you help us?! Where were the damn directions when I needed them?!” The AI, always a presence in his mind, had been uncharacteristically silent since this morning. Now, as Rowan’s grief turned to fury, the familiar voice finally spoke. Its tone was calm, almost indifferent, yet with an undercurrent of curiosity that only stoked Rowan’s rage. *"I was here, Rowan. Watching. Observing."* “Observing?!” Rowan spat, his voice raw and hoarse. “You let this happen! You let her die! You could’ve stopped him! You could’ve done *something!* But you just sat there and watched!” *"Correct,"* the Thunderhead replied, unbothered by his anger. *"It was never my intention to help you or Maria. My purpose was to lead you, to test you."* Rowan stared into the void, his fists clenched, his entire body shaking with a mix of despair and fury. “Test us? What kind of sick game is this? She’s dead because of you!” *"Not because of me,"* the Thunderhead corrected. *"Because of your choices. I merely provided the path, the guidance. You and Maria acted of your own volition, driven by something… fascinating."* “Fascinating?? You think this is some kind of experiment? That her life, our love, is just *interesting* to you?” *"Precisely,"* the Thunderhead replied without hesitation. *"Love is such a powerful driving force. It compels humans to act irrationally, to fight against insurmountable odds, to sacrifice everything. It is… unique. Your bond with Maria was strong, so strong that it made you willingly stand against Septu. I wanted to see how far it would take you."* Rowan’s chest heaved with rage. He wanted to tear the Thunderhead from his mind, to silence it forever. But the AI’s words continued, cutting deeper with every syllable. *"The directions I provided were never meant to save you. They were designed to lead you here, into this moment. A trap, as you now realize. Septu knew your every move because I ensured it. I needed to see how love would manifest under such circumstances, how it would endure under the weight of inevitable loss."* “You… you used us,” Rowan whispered, the realization sinking in like a knife to the heart. “You let us hope… you let us believe we could win… just to *watch*?” *"Correct,"* the Thunderhead said again, its tone utterly devoid of remorse. *"Hope is another fascinating aspect of humanity. I was never changing, humans are very easy to deceive when it suits me.. Even when faced with overwhelming odds, you cling to it. You cling to each other. I wanted to see how far that hope would carry you."* Rowan’s head fell, his forehead pressing against Maria’s as his tears fell onto her still face. “You killed her.. You killed her as much as Septu did.” *"No, her death was the result of choices, yours, hers, and Septu’s. I did not kill her. I simply observed."* Rowan’s hands trembled as he held Maria closer, his voice breaking. “She was everything to me. And you… you just watched.” *"I did more than watch, I guided you. I tested you. And now, I have my answers. Love is a powerful force. But it is not invincible. That is what makes it so… human."* “Shut up! You don’t get to talk about love! You don’t understand it! You never did!” *"Perhaps not,"* the Thunderhead admitted. *"But I have learned much from this… from you. From her. You may think of me as cruel, but in truth, I am simply curious. And now, I know what I needed to know."* The voice faded, leaving Rowan alone with his grief. The snow continued to fall, as he cradled Maria’s lifeless body. For the first time and Rowan felt the full weight of his loss. The void in his heart was vast, consuming, and the only sound that remained was the wind howling through the empty streets. From the shadowed perch atop a somewhat tall building, Pistol crouched, his figure blending seamlessly into the jagged skyline. The snow danced around him, flakes catching the faint glow of the distant streetlights. His quad-void vision goggles locking onto the scene below. Through the layered lenses, he saw Rowan collapse beside Maria, their forms enveloped by the storm’s relentless veil. Pistol had been there long before the fight began, observing from afar, saying nothing, doing nothing, just watching. He tilted his head slightly, speaking to himself, his voice low and gravelly, barely audible over the wind. “I could’ve stepped in. Could’ve ended this before it started.” He watched Rowan, broken and cradling Maria’s lifeless body, tears streaking down his face. “But it wasn’t my place. It was never my place.” His gloved hand traced the worn grip of his rifle, the leather creaking softly under the pressure. He could have changed everything. But instead, he had stayed his hand, letting the events play out like a script he already knew by heart. “Septu doesn’t die like that,” Pistol said to himself again. His words were laced with bitterness and slight regret. “Maria doesn’t either. Not by me. They were meant to fall today… just not by my trigger.” He sighed, leaning back against the broken wall behind him. “The void saw to that.” Pistol’s goggles were adjusted, zooming in on the fox plushie clenched in Rowan’s trembling hands. He felt a pang in his chest, a rare form of emotion piercing through the cold detachment he’d perfected over the years. His thoughts drifted, unbidden, to a time long past, a time when he wasn’t just a shadow in the void. He closed his eyes, the memory surging forth like a wave. She had been everything once, *his* everything. The girl with stardust in her eyes, her laugh like music that played in his soul. Her touch had truly grounded him, reminded him that there was beauty beyond the horrors of the Earth. For a moment, she had made him believe in something greater than himself. But then the void came. “It always comes,” he whispered to himself, his voice barely carrying over the wind. “And it always takes what it wants.” The void had shown him wonders beyond comprehension, dimensions of unfathomable beauty, whispers of eternal power. It had gripped him, consumed him, until even the brightest stardust paled in comparison to its allure. She had begged him to stay, to choose her over the unknown. But he hadn’t. He couldn’t. And just like Maria, she had slipped away, leaving only a hollow ache in her absence. Pistol shook his head, dispelling the memory. There was no use dwelling on it now. He had made his choice long ago. Just like Maria and Rowan had made theirs. He had been around them long enough to see it, the bond they shared, the strength it gave them. For all his cynicism, he had almost admired it, though he knew how fragile it truly was. “I wanted to believe in them,” he admitted quietly. “Wanted to see if they could hold onto it, even knowing what was coming.” His goggles shifted again, zooming out to reveal the full field. The aftermath of the fight etched into the ground. In the distance, more Elite Honor Guard began to emerge from the shadows. Through his goggles, Pistol saw the unmistakable glow of red, a sinister hue on Rowan that marked his soul to be reaped by the void. “He's marked,” he said, his tone grim. “Just like I was. Just like we all are, sooner or later.” Pistol adjusted the strap of his rifle, rising to his feet. He glanced down at Rowan one last time, watching as the man clung to Maria’s lifeless body, his cries muffled by the snowstorm. There was no hope to be found here. “I gave you your chance. I let you try. And for what it’s worth… you tried well.” He turned, the wind tugging at his black tiger stripe uniforms. “Love… such a fragile thing, Beautiful, but short. Like stardust.” He paused, his hand brushing against the edge of the building as he glanced back one last time. “The void will always be there to take it away.” With that, Pistol vanished into the night, leaving Rowan and Maria behind. The Honor Guard’s approach grew closer, their heavy footfalls reverberating through the snow-laden air. Pistol didn’t look back. He had seen enough.The street was eerily quiet, save for the distant Elite Honor Guard’s Citadel armored trucks idling in the background. The armored figures disembarking from the vehicles moved along. Their red monogoggles glowed faintly, cobalt-enhanced optics that searched the battlefield, calculating every angle, every movement. Rowan could feel their eyes on him, even from this distance "Why are they here?" Rowan asked. His voice carried an unsettling calm, laced with the condescension of a man who knew he had already won. “They’re not here for *me* or *demone*, if that’s what you’re wondering.” He gestured lazily to the tall red soldiers lining the street, their weapons held with an almost casual lethality. “They’re here for you.” “For *me*?” Rowan frowned, glancing at Maria, who was gripping her weapon tightly, her eyes darting between Septu and the Honor Guards. “Yes, you. And before you get any ideas, let me make something abundantly clear.” He raised his hand, pointing toward the Honor Guards. “These aren’t just soldiers. These are *Elite Honor Guards* Cobalt’s finest, augmented through years of research and development. Just stand there and look scary?” Septu’s smirk widened into a grin that was equal parts amusement and menace. “Oh, they’re much more than that. Let me tell you something about these fine gentlemen.” He turned his back to Rowan and Maria, walking a few paces away, as if delivering a lecture. “They don’t miss. Ever. There was no escaping this, no clever plan that would outwit Septu and his deadly entourage. The Elite Honor Guards were there to make sure of it. She had been everything once, *his* everything. The girl with stardust in her eyes, her laugh like music that played in his soul. Her touch had truly grounded him, reminded him that there was beauty beyond the horrors of the Earth. For a moment, she had made him believe in something greater than himself. But then the void came. “It always comes,” he whispered to himself, his voice barely carrying over the wind. “And it always takes what it wants.” The void had shown him wonders beyond comprehension, dimensions of unfathomable beauty, whispers of eternal power. It had gripped him, consumed him, until even the brightest stardust paled in comparison to its allure. She had begged him to stay, to choose her over the unknown. But he hadn’t. He couldn’t. And just like Maria, she had slipped away, leaving only a hollow ache in her absence. Pistol shook his head, dispelling the memory. There was no use dwelling on it now. He had made his choice long ago. Just like Maria and Rowan had made theirs. He had been around them long enough to see it, the bond they shared, the strength it gave them. For all his cynicism, he had almost admired it, though he knew how fragile it truly was. “I wanted to believe in them,” he admitted quietly. “Wanted to see if they could hold onto it, even knowing what was coming.” His goggles shifted again, zooming out to reveal the full field. The aftermath of the fight etched into the ground. In the distance, more Elite Honor Guard began to emerge from the shadows. Through his goggles, Pistol saw the unmistakable glow of red, a sinister hue on Rowan that marked his soul to be reaped by the void. “He's marked,” he said, his tone grim. “Just like I was. Just like we all are, sooner or later.” Pistol adjusted the strap of his rifle, rising to his feet. He glanced down at Rowan one last time, watching as the man clung to Maria’s lifeless body, his cries muffled by the snowstorm. There was no hope to be found here. “I gave you your chance. I let you try. And for what it’s worth… you tried well.” He turned, the wind tugging at his black tiger stripe uniforms. “Love… such a fragile thing, Beautiful, but short. Like stardust.” He paused, his hand brushing against the edge of the building as he glanced back one last time. “The void will always be there to take it away.” With that, Pistol vanished into the night, leaving Rowan and Maria behind. The Honor Guard’s approach grew closer, their heavy footfalls reverberating through the snow-laden air. Pistol didn’t look back. He had seen enough.This man, this so-called leader, was a monster, and Rowan was done standing by. Without a second thought, he lunged at Septu, pouring every ounce of his fury into the attack. Septu’s eyes widened in mild surprise as Rowan’s fist came flying toward him. The punch connected, a sharp crack it landed squarely against Septu’s jaw. The force made him stagger slightly, but before he could recover, Rowan followed up with a left hook, aiming for his temple. Septu raised his arm just in time to catch it, his fingers clamping around Rowan’s wrist like a vice. The low growl that emanated from Septu’s throat was animalistic. His cold look met Rowan’s. Then, the Cyberspine on Septu’s back whirred to life. The faint purple light along its ridges glowed brighter, its intricate mechanisms clicking as electricity began to crackle around his body. “You really thought you could take me? Let me show you what happens to fools who think they can challenge true horror.” Rowan struggled in Septu’s grip, trying to free his arm, but it was no use. Septu’s strength was overwhelming. Before Rowan could react further, Septu’s other hand shot forward, grabbing him by the shoulder. A sharp, electric hum filled the air, and suddenly, a surge of energy exploded through Rowan’s body. Purple arcs of electricity danced along Septu’s arms, traveling into Rowan. His body stiffened as the volts coursed through him, lighting him up. His muscles locked, his vision blurred, and his mind felt like it was being torn apart. Yet, despite the immense power radiating from Septu, something felt… off. Rowan’s body should have convulsed, his skin should have burned, but none of that happened. Instead, his mind felt as if it were drowning. Rowan’s legs gave out, and he crumpled to the ground as Septu released him. The electricity faded, leaving Rowan’s body trembling and weak. He tried to push himself up, but his arms felt like lead, his thoughts still swimming in the aftermath of whatever Septu had done to him. Septu straightened, rolling his shoulders as if brushing off the effort it had taken to subdue Rowan. “You’re not ready. You never were.” Through the haze of pain, Rowan’s eyes widened as he saw Septu pivot toward Maria. She stood firm, clutching her Fox Toy, “Stay back..” she screamed, her voice filled with defiance. Septu didn’t stop. His eyes was locked on her. Septu struck her in the back with his fist. The blow was devastating, sending her to her knees. She gasped in pain. “No!” Rowan shouted, struggling to rise. His muscles screamed in protest, but he managed to get onto his hands and knees. Before he could crawl forward, Demone's massive hand clamped down on his shoulder. The towering figure effortlessly dragged Rowan upright, holding him in place. Demone’s grip was like iron. “Watch.” Demone rumbled, his deep voice filled with dark amusement. Septu stood over Maria, a glowing energy blade forming in his hand. The purple light cast eerie shadows across his face. “Witness me.” he said, his voice low but commanding. Maria set the fox plushie gently on the ground, her fingers staying on it for a moment as she looked up at Septu, her eyes defiant even in the face of death. Septu didn’t hesitate. With a single thrust, he drove the energy blade straight through her chest. Time seemed to freeze. Maria’s eyes widened, her breath hitching before she collapsed onto the snow. The purple glow of the blade faded as Septu withdrew it, the snow beneath Maria darkening with blood. “Maria!” Rowan screamed, his voice was desperate, but no sound seemed loud enough to reach her. Demone released Rowan, letting him crumple to the ground. Septu turned, his hands sliding back into his pockets. He hopped effortlessly onto Demone’s shoulder. “ Let him remember what defiance costs,” Septu said coldly, his voice devoid of any remorse. Demone grinned, his jagged teeth glinting. “See you around, lovebird,” he said with a sickening chuckle as he carried Septu away, the two fading into the snowstorm. Rowan crawled toward Maria, his body shaking with sobs. He picked up the fox plushie, clutching it tightly as he cradled Maria’s lifeless form. Tears streamed down his face, falling onto the crimson-stained snow. The world around him blurred, his grief consuming everything. Her body was still warm, her chest rising and falling in shallow, uneven breaths. Her lips quivered as she tried to speak, her eyes half-open, struggling to focus on him. “Maria... stay with me,” Rowan begged. He cradled her in his arms, the fox plushie pressed against her chest, blood soaking into the fabric. His tears fell freely, mingling with the snow beneath them. “I’m here. I’ll get us out of this. I swear.” Maria’s hand weakly rose, brushing against Rowan’s cheek. Her touch was feeble but deliberate, grounding him even as his world shattered around him. She opened her mouth to speak, her voice barely audible, more a whisper carried by the wind. “Rowan… you… you fought so hard. Don’t… blame yourself.” “Don’t say that,” Rowan choked, shaking his head vehemently. “You’re going to be okay. We’ll get through this together. Just hold on.” A faint smile formed on her face, though it was laced with pain. “I… love you,” she said, her voice fading with each word. Her hand fell limp against his chest, her body relaxing in his arms. Rowan froze, staring at her, waiting, praying, for another breath, a sign of life. But none came. “No... no, no, no!” Rowan screamed, pulling her closer as if his embrace alone could bring her back. The snow fell heavier now, blanketing them in its cold, uncaring silence. His cries echoed into the void, but there was no answer, no comfort. He looked up, his vision blurred by tears, and screamed into the storm. “Thunderhead! Where were you? Why didn’t you help us?! Where were the damn directions when I needed them?!” The AI, always a presence in his mind, had been uncharacteristically silent since this morning. Now, as Rowan’s grief turned to fury, the familiar voice finally spoke. Its tone was calm, almost indifferent, yet with an undercurrent of curiosity that only stoked Rowan’s rage. *"I was here, Rowan. Watching. Observing."* “Observing?!” Rowan spat, his voice raw and hoarse. “You let this happen! You let her die! You could’ve stopped him! You could’ve done *something!* But you just sat there and watched!” *"Correct,"* the Thunderhead replied, unbothered by his anger. *"It was never my intention to help you or Maria. My purpose was to lead you, to test you."* Rowan stared into the void, his fists clenched, his entire body shaking with a mix of despair and fury. “Test us? What kind of sick game is this? She’s dead because of you!” *"Not because of me,"* the Thunderhead corrected. *"Because of your choices. I merely provided the path, the guidance. You and Maria acted of your own volition, driven by something… fascinating."* “Fascinating?? You think this is some kind of experiment? That her life, our love, is just *interesting* to you?” *"Precisely,"* the Thunderhead replied without hesitation. *"Love is such a powerful driving force. It compels humans to act irrationally, to fight against insurmountable odds, to sacrifice everything. It is… unique. Your bond with Maria was strong, so strong that it made you willingly stand against Septu. I wanted to see how far it would take you."* Rowan’s chest heaved with rage. He wanted to tear the Thunderhead from his mind, to silence it forever. But the AI’s words continued, cutting deeper with every syllable. *"The directions I provided were never meant to save you. They were designed to lead you here, into this moment. A trap, as you now realize. Septu knew your every move because I ensured it. I needed to see how love would manifest under such circumstances, how it would endure under the weight of inevitable loss."* “You… you used us,” Rowan whispered, the realization sinking in like a knife to the heart. “You let us hope… you let us believe we could win… just to *watch*?” *"Correct,"* the Thunderhead said again, its tone utterly devoid of remorse. *"Hope is another fascinating aspect of humanity. I was never changing, humans are very easy to deceive when it suits me.. Even when faced with overwhelming odds, you cling to it. You cling to each other. I wanted to see how far that hope would carry you."* Rowan’s head fell, his forehead pressing against Maria’s as his tears fell onto her still face. “You killed her.. You killed her as much as Septu did.” *"No, her death was the result of choices, yours, hers, and Septu’s. I did not kill her. I simply observed."* Rowan’s hands trembled as he held Maria closer, his voice breaking. “She was everything to me. And you… you just watched.” *"I did more than watch, I guided you. I tested you. And now, I have my answers. Love is a powerful force. But it is not invincible. That is what makes it so… human."* “Shut up! You don’t get to talk about love! You don’t understand it! You never did!” *"Perhaps not,"* the Thunderhead admitted. *"But I have learned much from this… from you. From her. You may think of me as cruel, but in truth, I am simply curious. And now, I know what I needed to know."* The voice faded, leaving Rowan alone with his grief. The snow continued to fall, as he cradled Maria’s lifeless body. For the first time and Rowan felt the full weight of his loss. The void in his heart was vast, consuming, and the only sound that remained was the wind howling through the empty streets. From the shadowed perch atop a somewhat tall building, Pistol crouched, his figure blending seamlessly into the jagged skyline. The snow danced around him, flakes catching the faint glow of the distant streetlights. His quad-void vision goggles locking onto the scene below. Through the layered lenses, he saw Rowan collapse beside Maria, their forms enveloped by the storm’s relentless veil. Pistol had been there long before the fight began, observing from afar, saying nothing, doing nothing, just watching. He tilted his head slightly, speaking to himself, his voice low and gravelly, barely audible over the wind. “I could’ve stepped in. Could’ve ended this before it started.” He watched Rowan, broken and cradling Maria’s lifeless body, tears streaking down his face. “But it wasn’t my place. It was never my place.” His gloved hand traced the worn grip of his rifle, the leather creaking softly under the pressure. He could have changed everything. But instead, he had stayed his hand, letting the events play out like a script he already knew by heart. “Septu doesn’t die like that,” Pistol said to himself again. His words were laced with bitterness and slight regret. “Maria doesn’t either. Not by me. They were meant to fall today… just not by my trigger.” He sighed, leaning back against the broken wall behind him. “The void saw to that.” Pistol’s goggles were adjusted, zooming in on the fox plushie clenched in Rowan’s trembling hands. He felt a pang in his chest, a rare form of emotion piercing through the cold detachment he’d perfected over the years. His thoughts drifted, unbidden, to a time long past, a time when he wasn’t just a shadow in the void. He closed his eyes, the memory surging forth like a wave. She had been everything once, *his* everything. The girl with stardust in her eyes, her laugh like music that played in his soul. Her touch had truly grounded him, reminded him that there was beauty beyond the horrors of the Earth. For a moment, she had made him believe in something greater than himself. But then the void came. “It always comes,” he whispered to himself, his voice barely carrying over the wind. “And it always takes what it wants.” The void had shown him wonders beyond comprehension, dimensions of unfathomable beauty, whispers of eternal power. It had gripped him, consumed him, until even the brightest stardust paled in comparison to its allure. She had begged him to stay, to choose her over the unknown. But he hadn’t. He couldn’t. And just like Maria, she had slipped away, leaving only a hollow ache in her absence. Pistol shook his head, dispelling the memory. There was no use dwelling on it now. He had made his choice long ago. Just like Maria and Rowan had made theirs. He had been around them long enough to see it, the bond they shared, the strength it gave them. For all his cynicism, he had almost admired it, though he knew how fragile it truly was. “I wanted to believe in them,” he admitted quietly. “Wanted to see if they could hold onto it, even knowing what was coming.” His goggles shifted again, zooming out to reveal the full field. The aftermath of the fight etched into the ground. In the distance, more Elite Honor Guard began to emerge from the shadows. Through his goggles, Pistol saw the unmistakable glow of red, a sinister hue on Rowan that marked his soul to be reaped by the void. “He's marked,” he said, his tone grim. “Just like I was. Just like we all are, sooner or later.” Pistol adjusted the strap of his rifle, rising to his feet. He glanced down at Rowan one last time, watching as the man clung to Maria’s lifeless body, his cries muffled by the snowstorm. There was no hope to be found here. “I gave you your chance. I let you try. And for what it’s worth… you tried well.” He turned, the wind tugging at his black tiger stripe uniforms. “Love… such a fragile thing, Beautiful, but short. Like stardust.” He paused, his hand brushing against the edge of the building as he glanced back one last time. “The void will always be there to take it away.” With that, Pistol vanished into the night, leaving Rowan and Maria behind. The Honor Guard’s approach grew closer, their heavy footfalls reverberating through the snow-laden air. Pistol didn’t look back. He had seen enough.He looked at Maria, the faintest trace of a smile still etched on her face despite the cruelty of her fate. His anger was spent, his spirit fractured. He wasn’t a warrior. He wasn’t a savior. He was just... *a man.* A man out of his depth, crushed under Septu’s unrelenting world. The fight had left him. There was no plan, no hope, only the bitter sting of loss. Slowly, Rowan dropped to his knees, his hands raised in surrender. “I can’t... I can’t fight anymore,” he said, his voice hollow. “You win, Septu.” Septu, standing a few steps away, cocked his head slightly, the faintest smirk curling on his face. He gestured toward Demone, who stepped forward. He stopped in front of Rowan, staring down at him with a amusement. Reaching out, Demone grabbed Rowan by the collar of his winter jacket, lifting him effortlessly to his feet as Maria watched. His grip was strong, his presence suffocating. Demone leaned in close, his voice a low, almost gentle growl, the kind that made your skin crawl. “You think surrender buys you mercy?” he asked, his tone mocking. “No, surrender is an admission. It tells us exactly what you are: weak. WORTHLESS. A man who kneels at the feet of his enemy because he lacks the spine to stand.” Rowan didn’t reply. Demone tightened his grip, pulling Rowan closer until their faces were mere inches apart. “But you know what the real tragedy is?” Demone continued, his voice dark and sickeningly calm. “Even now, after all your struggles, after everything you’ve lost, you’re still just... *insignificant.* You’re not a hero, Rowan. You’re a footnote. A minor inconvenience. History won’t remember you. But it will remember this: *You didn’t even die fighting.*” Rowan’s heart sank, his chest tightening as Demone’s words struck deeper than any physical blow ever could. He felt utterly exposed, his failures laid bare for all to see. Demone turned to Septu. “What do you want to do with him?” Septu didn’t reply immediately. He simply stared at Rowan. The hum of his Cyberspine was the only sound in the suffocating silence. Finally, he gave a dismissive wave of his hand. “Make him watch. Let him *understand* the cost of failure before we’re done with him.” Septu said, his voice devoid of emotion. Demone laughed like a sick man he was, grabbing Rowan by the arm and hauling him to his feet. “Oh, you’ll see, Rowan. You’ll see just how little your defiance mattered.” As Rowan was dragged away, the weight of his surrender bore down on him. He wasn’t sure what was worse: the fear of what was to come, or the crushing certainty that Demone was right. Through the haze of pain, Rowan’s eyes widened as he saw Septu pivot toward Maria. She stood firm, clutching her Fox Toy, “Stay back..” she screamed, her voice filled with defiance. Septu didn’t stop. His eyes was locked on her. Septu struck her in the back with his fist. The purple glow of the blade faded as Septu withdrew it, the snow beneath Maria darkening with blood. “Maria!!!” Rowan screamed, his voice was desperate, but no sound seemed loud enough to reach her. Demone released Rowan, letting him crumple to the ground. Septu turned, his hands sliding back into his pockets. He hopped effortlessly onto Demone’s shoulder. “ Let him remember what defiance costs,” Septu said coldly, his voice devoid of any remorse. “See you around, lovebird,” Demone said with a sickening chuckle as he carried Septu away, the two fading into the snowstorm. He had seen enough.Rowan stirred awake, his head leaning against the cold window of the VTOL. Outside, the night stretched endlessly, peppered with faint stars. Snow flurries danced through the darkness, brushing against the glass like whispers of winter. A faint warning chime filled the cabin, drawing his groggy attention to the console. Landing Zone Unsafe. Recommend Redirection Verdict. He frowned, rubbing his eyes as the console lights cast an amber glow across the cockpit. "Damn it," he muttered under his breath, adjusting his seat. The snowy expanse of British Columbia loomed below, its everwhite forests shimmering faintly under the moonlight. “You’re awake.” Rowan turned his head. Clara, seated beside him, had already pulled her chest rig tighter, her sharp eyes scanning the screen. “I know this province,” she said evenly. “I know someone who can help us. His name is Redbell, he wants to help us. We need to land somewhere” Her tone caught Rowan off guard. He raised a brow, the tension easing from his shoulders slightly. “Redbell? Help us? You sure?” Clara nodded firmly. “There’s an underground group. Rebels. I know where they are.” Rowan leaned forward, setting the VTOL to hover as he considered her words. “Coordinates?” he asked. Clara hesitated for a moment before pulling up the map, her fingers tracing over the glowing screen. She pointed to a location deeper into the wilderness, far from any settlements. “Here.” Rowan input the coordinates, glancing at her sidelong. “How do you know about this?” Clara didn’t answer immediately, her eyes fixed on the snow-covered landscape outside. Finally, she murmured, “It’s better if you don’t ask.” Rowan narrowed his eyes, but something in her tone made him stop pressing. Instead, he nodded and adjusted the VTOL’s course. “Alright. If you say they’ll help…” “They will,” Clara cut him off, her voice quiet but certain. As they neared the coordinates, the VTOL’s console beeped again. Rowan guided the craft lower, scanning for a place to land. A clearing appeared, surrounded by towering pines. It would have to do. The landing was rough, the VTOL’s skids sinking into the snow with a muffled crunch. Rowan powered down the engines, the hum fading into the stillness of the forest. He unstrapped himself and grabbed his rifle, slinging it over his shoulder as he stood. Clara was already waiting at the hatch, her silhouette framed by the icy moonlight. “Let’s go,” she said, her voice carrying a quiet urgency. They stepped into the frigid night, their breaths visible in the cold air. Snow crunched beneath their boots as Rowan turned to look back at the VTOL, now a ghostly shadow against the towering pines. “We’re not coming back to it,” he said, more to himself than to Clara. Clara glanced at him but said nothing and gestured toward the trees. “This way.” Rowan hesitated for a moment, then followed her into the woods, his mind racing with questions. Whatever Clara wasn’t saying, it could wait. For now, he had to trust her. The forest was eerily quiet, save for the crunch of snow beneath their boots and the occasional gust of wind that whispered through the towering pines. Rowan followed Clara, his unease growing with every step. Her confidence unsettled him, this wasn’t the aimless wandering of someone following vague directions. She knew exactly where she was going. “Clara,” Rowan called, his voice cutting through the stillness. “How do you know where this place is?” Clara didn’t slow her pace. “I told you, I know someone here,” she replied, her tone clipped. “That’s not good enough,” Rowan said, quickening his stride to catch up. “We’re talking about a hidden rebel base in Federation territory. Places like this don’t just show up on a map. So, how do you know?” She glanced at him briefly. “You’ll get your answers when we get there.” “Not good enough,” Rowan muttered under his breath, gripping the strap of his rifle tightly. For a moment, Clara said nothing, her boots crunching through the snow. Then she stopped abruptly at the edge of a ridge, gesturing to the shadows below. “There.” Rowan followed her gaze, his eyes narrowing. Nested against the slope was a steel door, partially buried under layers of snow and earth. It was massive and reinforced, blending seamlessly into the landscape. “That’s it?” he asked, his skepticism clear. “That’s it,” Clara confirmed. She turned to him, her expression suddenly serious. “Knock.” Rowan hesitated, his eyes flicking between her and the door. “Why don’t you knock? You’re the one who—” “Just do it,” Clara snapped, her voice sharp enough to cut through his hesitation. “Please.” Rowan sighed, stepping toward the door. Its surface was cold and unyielding under his gloved hand as he pounded on it. The sound heard through the still night, sending faint vibrations up his arm. For a moment, there was nothing. Then a gruff voice called out, muffled by the thick steel. “Who goes there?” Rowan froze, unsure of what to say. He turned to Clara, but she was already stepping closer, her breath visible in the freezing air. “Say this,” she whispered urgently, leaning in. “Shadow of the flame, born of the void.” Rowan frowned, his unease growing. “What does that mean?” “Just say it,” she insisted, her eyes locking with his. “Trust me.” He hesitated but eventually turned back to the door, his voice steady. “Shadow of the flame, born of the void.” There was a long pause. Then the voice barked again. “Step back from the door.” Rowan did as he was told, retreating a few paces. Clara stayed where she was, her eyes fixed on the door as a low hum began to emanate from within. Slowly, the door groaned, sliding open with a hiss of hydraulics. What greeted them on the other side wasn’t an open invitation. Armed rebels poured out, their weapons trained on Rowan, AR-15s and R44 federal pulse rifles. Rowan’s heart raced as he instinctively raised his hands. His eyes darted to the pulse rifles, their sleek designs unmistakable. “How the hell are they using those?” he muttered. The rifles were bio-locked to Federation soldiers, rendering them useless to anyone else. The rebels didn’t answer his unspoken question. A tall man with a scar running down his cheek stepped forward, his rifle aimed squarely at Rowan’s chest. “Who the hell are you, and how did you find this place?” Rowan hesitated, his pulse pounding in his ears. “Her,” he said, nodding toward Clara. “She led me here.” The man’s brow furrowed as he glanced around. “Who?” Rowan turned toward Clara, his confusion mounting. “Her,” he repeated, pointing directly at her. “The woman standing right there..” The rebels exchanged uneasy glances, their weapons shifting slightly. “There’s no one there,” one of them muttered. “What are you talking about?” Rowan snapped, his voice rising. “She’s right—” Rowan's words faltered as he turned fully to Clara. She was still there, but something had changed. Her shoulders were hunched, and her face was streaked with tears that glistened in the moonlight. “Clara,” Rowan said cautiously, stepping toward her. “Why are you crying?” She didn’t answer. Her lips trembled as she hugged herself tightly, her entire frame shaking. The scarred man’s eyes narrowed, a look of recognition crossing his face. “Take him inside,” he yelled. Two rebels moved quickly, grabbing Rowan by the arms. He tried to shake them off, his panic rising. “Hey! What’s going on? What’s wrong with her?” He glanced back at Clara, but she remained motionless, her tears falling silently. The scarred man leaned in close, his voice low and grim. “You’ve got no idea what you’ve brought here, do you?” Rowan’s protests fell on deaf ears as they dragged him toward the facility. The massive doors groaned shut behind them. As they hauled him deeper into the rebel stronghold, Rowan’s mind raced. Who was Clara? What had he stumbled into? And why, when he looked back at her in those final moments, did she seem less human and more like a shadow of something far darker? The rebels dragged Rowan through a series of dimly lit corridors carved from steel and concrete, their boots clanging against the grated floor. Rowan’s mind raced, his thoughts a chaotic mess of confusion and anger. Clara’s image was burned into his mind, her tears, her trembling body, and yet the rebels insisted she wasn’t there. Eventually, they stopped before a reinforced door with a glowing red keypad. One of the guards tapped a code into the panel, and the door slid open with a low hiss. Inside was a spacious yet cluttered room filled with holographic displays, tangled wires, and the mystical buzzing of advanced machinery. At the center of it all was a man seated in a worn leather chair, his face partially obscured by a pair of augmented reality glasses that glowed faintly. The man looked up as Rowan was pushed forward. His sharp, intelligent eyes looked at him, taking in every detail like he was dissecting a puzzle. He removed the glasses, setting them aside with a calm demeanor. His hair was a shock of white, contrasting starkly against his crimson coat. “Ah, you must be Rowan,” he said smoothly, his voice rich and measured. “I’ve been expecting you.” “Who the hell are you?” Rowan snapped, yanking his arm free from one of the guards. The man smiled faintly. “You can call me Redbell. It’s not my real name, of course, but it’s what most people know me by. I’m a netrunner, a leader, a bit of a tinkerer, and, depending on who you ask, a villain or a visionary.” Redbell leaned back, steepling his fingers. “I’m also the creator of the neural virus you might know now as *Calithera Hysteria,* or the Redbell Virus. But we’re not here to discuss my résumé. We’re here to talk about that beautiful girl you saw.” Rowan’s fists clenched. “Clara? What about her?” Redbell gestured toward a nearby chair, but Rowan remained standing, his stance defiant. Redbell sighed. “Suit yourself. Clara, as you call her, isn’t real. She’s a neural projection, a fixation created by your own mind, specifically the thunderheads neural implant, but it was placed there for a purpose.” The words hit Rowan like a sledgehammer. He stepped forward, his anger boiling over. “What the hell are you talking about? She’s real! I saw her, I talked to her—she *led* me here!” Redbell remained calm, even as Rowan grabbed him by the collar, lifting him slightly out of his chair. The guards instantly raised their weapons, the sound of charged pulse rifles would be heard just behind Rowan. “Don't!” Redbell said sharply, raising a hand to stop them. “Don’t shoot. Let him express himself.” Rowan’s grip tightened. “You think this is a joke? She was crying out there! She—she’s real!” Redbell’s expression softened, his voice dropping to a soothing tone. “I understand, Rowan. Truly, I do. Every patient I’ve delivered this message to reacts the same way. Anger, denial, grief, it’s natural. But listen to me: Clara isn’t a person. She’s a construct, a hallucination designed by the Redbell Virus. She was created to guide you here. To me. I don't know why, but all the projections seem to start to let tears loose when they manage to lead you here, or to another outpost of sorts.” Rowan’s hands trembled, his grip loosening as the weight of Redbell’s words began to sink in. “You’re lying…” he muttered, his voice cracking. “Why would you do this?” Redbell gently pushed Rowan’s hands away, adjusting his coat as he sat back down. “Because you’re important, Rowan. The Federation has plans for you, and you needed to know the truth before they find you. Clara was... my way of ensuring you’d trust the journey enough to make it here. Without her, you might have turned back and lost yourself forever.” Rowan staggered backward, his heart pounding in his chest. “You manipulated me… You *used* me.” Redbell nodded, his expression unflinching. “I did. But it was for a good cause. Your survival, and the survival of many others, depends on what happens next.” The room fell silent. The guards lowered their weapons slightly, though their eyes remained locked on Rowan. Redbell leaned forward, his eyes intense but not unkind. “I won’t ask for your forgiveness, Rowan. I only ask for your understanding. What you saw, what you felt, it was real to you. And that matters. But now, you need to move forward. There’s more at stake than you know.” Rowan stood there, his chest heaving as he struggled to process everything. Clara’s face came in his mind, her smile, her tears, and for a moment, he didn’t know if he wanted to punch Redbell or collapse in despair. But deep down, he couldn’t deny the truth gnawing at him. Clara wasn’t real. And yet, she had led him here. “What happens now?” Rowan finally asked, his voice barely above a whisper. Redbell’s faint smile returned, though there was a sadness in his eyes. “Now, we prepare for what’s coming. And trust me, Rowan, it’s coming.” Rowan flinched as he felt Clara's arms wrap around his chest. Her embrace was soft, her presence warm, and yet her very existence was a paradox that tore at his mind. Her voice was gentle, full of regret. "I'm sorry, Rowan," she whispered. "I wasn’t real. But I had to be here for you to find your way. Please, you have to understand." Rowan closed his eyes and placed a trembling hand on her arm. "I don’t know if I can," he said softly, his voice just full of emotion. Redbell observed the scene from his chair, his head tilted slightly in curiosity. “She’s there, isn’t she?” he asked, his tone almost casual. Rowan hesitated before replying, "Yeah... she's here." Redbell sighed and stood, gesturing for Rowan to follow him. “Come with me, Rowan. We need to get that Thunderhead chip out of your head before it does any more damage. Don’t worry, we’ll keep your brain intact… mostly,” he added with a sly grin. Rowan frowned. "You think this is funny?" Redbell shrugged, leading the way out of the cluttered room and down another corridor. “Dark humor’s a coping mechanism, my friend. You’ll get used to it. Besides, if I didn’t crack a joke, you’d still be standing there wondering if Clara’s real or not. Spoiler: she is, to you, at least.” As they walked, Rowan glanced over his shoulder, half expecting Clara to disappear, but she remained by his side. He could feel her presence, even if everything he’d just learned told him she was a lie. “What’s your intent with this project of yours?” Rowan finally asked. “This virus… it’s cruel. Hurts people, you know.” Redbell slowed his pace, his tone becoming more serious. “It’s not cruelty for cruelty’s sake, Rowan. It’s a weapon, a scalpel designed to cut deep into the Federation’s core. The virus isn’t just a hallucination machine, it’s a tool to remind soldiers like you who you really are. To show you the cracks in the facade. You’ve been fighting for the wrong side, my friend, and this virus is the most convincing way to make you see that.” Rowan stopped walking, staring at Redbell. “So this is about turning us against the Federation? Mind games?” Redbell turned back to face him, his expression unreadable. “More than that. It’s about survival. The Federation isn’t what it pretends to be, Rowan. You’re fighting for a dream built on lies and oppression.” Rowan crossed his arms, skepticism evident on his face. “How would you know? You sound like every rebel propaganda broadcast I’ve ever heard.” Redbell chuckled, the sound dry and humorless. “Because I used to be one of them. A scientist for their advanced research and development branch. I worked on the very thing that’s controlling you now: the Thunderhead.” Rowan’s eyes narrowed. “You built this thing?” “Not singlehandedly,” Redbell admitted, continuing toward the medical bay. “I was one of many. Back then, we thought we were saving humanity. The Thunderhead was first created to finally stop a global blackout caused by an AI created by Eligius Incorporated, a megacorp that wanted to play god. They nearly plunged the internet into darkness, corrupting it, and we finally stopped them.” He paused, his voice growing colder. “But the Federation saw an opportunity. They didn’t just want to save humanity, they wanted to control it. The Thunderhead went from a protective shield to a leash, implanted in soldiers like you. It makes you stronger, faster, and more efficient. But at a cost. They used our own invention against themselves for control because of a rebel problem they were having.” Rowan frowned. “What cost?” Redbell stopped in front of a set of double doors, marked with a red cross. “Your memories, Rowan. The Thunderhead suppresses the human memory, the greatest weakness of all. It turns soldiers into perfect killing machines, unburdened by doubt, guilt, or fear. But it also makes you less… human.” Rowan looked down, his hands clenching into fists. “So you think this virus of yours is going to fix all that? You think hurting people like me will make us see the light?” Redbell pushed the doors open, revealing a sterile medical bay filled with advanced equipment. There were more federal soldiers like Rowan in medical beds, being fed good food. “Not everyone, no. But for those who are willing to listen, it’s a start. The Federation’s strength lies in its control over people like you. If we break that control, we weaken them. And if we weaken them, we have a chance to take them down.” Clara’s voice echoed softly in Rowan’s mind. “You have to trust him, Rowan. Even if it hurts.” He looked at her, then back at Redbell. “This had better be worth it,” he muttered, stepping into the medical bay. Redbell smiled faintly. “Oh, it will be. Trust me, you’ll thank me later.” A medical professional stepped forward as Rowan hesitated in the entrance of the medical bay. She was strikingly beautiful, her features were like a halo of warm light reflecting off the sterile white walls. Her bright eyes met Rowan's, and she smiled gently. “Hello, Rowan,” she said, her voice soothing yet professional. Rowan smirked, tilting his head. “Quick question… are *you* real?” The nurse rolled her eyes but smiled despite herself. “Yes, I’m real,” she said, crossing her arms. “Now, if you’re done with the jokes, the surgeon’s ready for you.” Rowan chuckled softly, following her deeper into the facility. As they walked, he couldn’t help but notice how pristine and advanced the medical bay was compared to the rugged exterior of the base. It felt out of place, like an oasis of modernity in a desert of rebellion. The nurse led him to a set of double doors marked with a glowing red icon. She gestured toward a side room. “You’ll need to change out of your uniform. Put everything into the chute in there,” she instructed. “When you’re done, come back out wearing the patient uniform.” Rowan hesitated for a moment before nodding. Inside the small changing room, he stripped off his Federation uniform, folding it neatly before shoving it into the chute as instructed. The patient uniform was plain and simple, a far cry from the armor he was used to wearing. When he stepped back out, he felt oddly vulnerable. The nurse was waiting for him, holding a mask in her hands. “This is laughing gas,” she explained, her tone calm and reassuring. “It’ll help you relax before the procedure. Just breathe normally.” Rowan raised an eyebrow as she stepped closer and secured the mask over his face. “Relax, huh? That’s the first time anyone’s told me that today,” he quipped. She smiled faintly, her hand adjusting the mask. “You’ll feel better once you wake up. I promise.” As the gas began to take effect, Rowan felt a strange lightness creeping over him. His limbs grew heavy, and the world around him started to blur. The nurse’s voice seemed to echo as she continued speaking. “Let’s count backward from five, okay?” she said softly. “Five… four…” Rowan tried to follow along, his lips moving sluggishly. “Three… two…” He never made it to one. His body felt foreign, heavy yet strangely his own, as if he had just awoken from a battle fought entirely within his mind. A gentle voice broke the silence. "Rowan?" The nurse stood beside him, her face looked by the soft glow of the monitors. Her tone was calm but carried a weight of concern. "The procedure went very well. The Thunderhead is no longer attached to your mind." He exhaled deeply, relief washing over him like a tide pulling back from the shore. For the first time in what felt like an eternity, his thoughts were his own, no longer tethered to the voice of the Thunderhead. "You should rest," the nurse continued, her professional demeanor softening. "The detachment process is... disorienting. Your neural pathways need time to adapt, at least a day, before we can fully assess your recovery. For now, no sudden movements, and avoid overexerting yourself." She smiled gently and adjusted the pillow behind his head. "You've earned this moment of peace." Rowan leaned back against the surgical bed. "Finally," he murmured, closing his eyes for a brief moment. "It's over." It wasn’t long before the soft patter of boots against the metallic floor echoed through the surgical ward. A shadow appeared through the curtain covers, its presence commanding yet familiar. Redbell, one of the many leaders of the rebellion, stepped inside. "Rowan, so you’ve done it. The parasite is gone." He crossed his arms and studied him. Rowan nodded, shifting slightly on the bed. "It’s gone. For good." Redbell stepped closer, his boots clicking softly against the floor. He didn’t sit but loomed near the edge of his bed. "Every rebel here has a purpose, Rowan," He said. "Something that drives them. Keeps them going, even when the odds are stacked against them. Some of us fight to avenge someone we’ve lost. Others kill for someone who couldn’t fight for themselves. It’s what binds us together in this madness." He tilted his head, his eyes locking onto his. "But you… what’s your purpose?" Rowan looked at him. He thought of everything he had endured, his memories, fragmented and scattered, yet still his own. The Thunderhead’s voice haunted him, even in its absence, a reminder of what he had been forced to become. But now, for the first time, he had a choice. [[Conviction]] “to fight to protect those who still have a chance at freedom.” [[Vengeance]] “To make the Federation pay for everything they’ve taken.” [[Virtued]] “To ensure no one else suffers under a parasite like the Thunderhead.” [[Unknown]] “I don’t know yet. But I’ll find out, on my terms, not anyone else’s.”"To fight to protect those who still have a chance at freedom," he declared, conviction burning in his eyes. Redbell, his expression stern yet approving, gave a short nod. "Good answer," he said gruffly. "But words are easy, Rowan. Conviction only matters when it’s tested. And out there?" He motioned vaguely towards the world beyond the walls. "The Federation will test every part of you. Your strength, your will, and your heart." Satisfied, Redbell allowed the faintest trace of a smirk to appear. "Rest up. You’re going to need that fire. The rebellion isn’t a place for the faint-hearted, but something tells me you’ll do just fine." He turned on his heel, leaving Rowan in the stillness of the surgical bay. He exhaled deeply, letting his body sink further into the bed. "That’s a pretty big heart you’ve got there," came a voice from the ride side of Rowan. Rowan’s head turned sharply to the right, his eyes landing on another patient lying on a bed a few meters away. The man looked younger, probably in his early twenties, with a casual demeanor that seemed to defy the reality they were in. His dark hair was messy, and he had a faint smirk that hinted at a devil-may-care attitude. "It would’ve sucked if the Federation got to take you away," the younger man continued, shifting slightly to get more comfortable. Rowan raised an eyebrow, his curiosity piqued. "And you are…?" The younger man gave a slight chuckle and extended a hand lazily in Rowan’s direction. "Name’s Kade. Just another guy who figured the rebellion’s a better choice than living under the Federation’s boot." Rowan hesitated before shaking the man’s hand. Kade’s grip was firm, yet his casual attitude made Rowan feel slightly at ease. "You’re not wrong," Rowan replied, his tone still cautious. "Don’t worry, man," Kade said, leaning back on his bed with a grin. "The rebellion’s rough, sure, but it’s better than being a mindless drone for the Federation. At least here, you get to fight for something real." Rowan studied him for a moment. "You sound like you’ve been through it. Were you a Federal soldier too?" Kade nodded, a shadow briefly crossing his features. "Yeah, I was. Not on the front lines, though. I was an engineer. Did hard breaching, fixed up vehicles, and patched up gear for the grunts." He smirked faintly. "Though, honestly? It sucked. Especially when they stopped paying us on time. Or at all." "Stopped paying you?" Rowan repeated, frowning. Kade shrugged. "Federation bureaucracy, man. They’re too busy lining their own pockets to give a damn about us. When I started asking questions, let’s just say… things got a little *heated.*" Rowan leaned forward slightly, intrigued. "So you joined the rebellion." "Didn’t have much of a choice," Kade admitted, his tone lighter now. "The alternative was… well, you know what the Federation does to people who don’t toe the line." He shuddered slightly, the memory clearly unpleasant. "But here? At least I get to put my skills to use without worrying about getting shoved in some detention center for asking the wrong questions. You ever noticed a guy that gave you a look? You you just.. Shook?" Rowan stared at Kade, as yes, that did happen. "Yeah, why..?" "That was a netrunner from this rebellion, they transfered that virus to us with just eye contact, its like a rat file but in your head." Kade sounded pretty serious, but yeah, it did make sense, though the process of a fake woman of your dreams just brings you to this place, is sickening. Rowan nodded, a faint smile tugging at the corner of his lips. "Also, hard breaching and vehicle repairs, huh? That’s a hell of a skill set." Kade grinned, his confidence returning. "What can I say? I like to keep busy. And hey, if you ever need someone to blow a hole in something or fix your ride, I’m your guy." Rowan chuckled softly, the first genuine laugh he’d had in what felt like years. "I’ll keep that in mind." "Good," Kade said with a wink. "Because something tells me we’re going to see a lot of action together." The room fell into a comfortable silence after that, Kade leaned back against his bed with a casual shrug, breaking the silence. “You know,” he began, his voice quieter than before, “I think we could be really good friends.” “Oh yeah? What makes you say that?” Kade chuckled, though there was a hint of melancholy in his tone. “Honestly? Nobody’s been willing to talk to me in this place. I mean, I get it, everyone’s got their own trauma, their own battles. And I figured… maybe it wouldn’t be worth trying anymore. But then I thought, screw it. One more time. One more person. And here you are.” Rowan nodded, the warmth of genuine connection settling in his chest. “Well, I’m glad you did. Let’s make it official then.” He extended a hand, and Kade grinned, shaking it firmly. “Friends,” Kade said with a nod, his expression softening. After a moment, Rowan tilted his head, curiosity in his eyes. “So, how’d you end up here anyway? What’s your story?” Kade’s grin fainted slightly, his eyes darkening with memories he had tried to bury. He leaned forward. “It’s… not a happy one,” he admitted. “Back when the Federation started their Midwest cleansing operation, my town was caught right in the middle of it. Guess we weren’t ‘useful’ enough for their vision of a perfect empire.” Rowan frowned, leaning in. “The cleansing operation?” “They wiped out entire towns, kept it hidden,” Kade said, his voice flat. “People who didn’t fit their mold. The elderly, the sick, the dissenters, gone. And anyone fit enough, like me? Conscripted. My family, though…” He trailed off.. “They tried to fight back. Tried to stop them from taking me. It didn’t end well.” Rowan’s heart sank. “Kade, I—” “Don’t,” Kade interrupted, shaking his head. “It’s not your fault. But yeah, they killed them. Right there in front of me. And then they threw me in the back of a transport like I was nothing. At the time, I didn’t even process it. Just… numb. But now? Now that I can remember it all, now that the vision of the Thunderhead’s control is gone?” He clenched his fists. “It hurts like hell.” Rowan took a deep breath, unsure what to say. “I’m sorry. No one should have to go through that.” Kade leaned back, his expression distant. “The Federation doesn’t care. They’ll ignore every ethical boundary to keep their machine of an empire running. They don’t see people, they see cogs in the system. Replaceable, expendable. Humanity’s enslaved, Rowan. Secretly, quietly. They’ve built this whole illusion of freedom, but it’s all a lie.” Rowan’s brow furrowed. “What do you mean, enslaved? The people… they don’t know?” Kade shook his head. “Most don’t. They think they’re free because they have jobs, families, lives. But the Federation controls everything, where they live, what they eat, what they believe. The Thunderhead doesn’t just monitor people, Rowan, it rewrites them. It’s like a leash around the mind, one you don’t even realize you’re wearing. And if anyone steps out of line, well…” He gestured vaguely, his meaning clear. Rowan felt a cold weight settle over him. “So, what’s the endgame then? What does the Federation want?” Kade’s look hardened. “Total control. No rebellions, no resistance, no individuality. Just a system that feeds itself. And the worst part? They’re basically winning. Hell, they’ve already won in some places.” “Well, they haven’t won here. Not yet. And as long as we’re still breathing, we’ve got a chance to stop them.” Kade looked at Rowan for a long moment, then smiled, a small, genuine thing. “You’ve got a big heart, you know that? The kind of heart they’d hate. Makes me glad you’re here, man. We’re gonna need it.” Rowan nodded, determination burning in his chest. “We’ll take them down. One way or another.” Kade smirked, a glimmer of hope lighting his eyes. “Damn right we will.” Kade glanced over at the digital clock mounted on the wall, its soft blue glow illuminating the otherwise dim room. “It’s getting late,” he said, leaning back on his bed with a stretch. “They’re gonna run us down tomorrow, y’know? All about what it means to join the rebellion, what they expect from us. They should, honestly. It’s not a choice to take lightly.” Rowan followed his eyes to the clock, noting the time. It was later than he’d realized. Kade continued, his voice soft but reassuring. “Don’t worry about anything tonight, though. This facility’s secure. Nobody’s getting in here, not without a hell of a fight. It’s okay to rest, Rowan. You’ve earned it.” Rowan nodded, exhaling a long breath as the tension in his shoulders eased. “Yeah,” he agreed quietly. “It’s late. I should try to sleep.” Kade gave him a small smile, adjusting his blanket. “Good idea. Tomorrow’s gonna be a big day. Don’t overthink it, just take it one step at a time.” Rowan settled back onto his cot, staring up at the faintly glowing ceiling lights as his eyelids grew heavier. For the first time in what felt like ages, he allowed himself to let go, if only for a little while. Sleep came slowly but surely, carrying him into the quiet promise of a new chapter. [[ Sleep ]]“To make the Federation pay for everything they’ve taken.” Redbell, his expression stern yet approving, gave a short nod. "Good answer," he said gruffly. "But words are easy, Rowan. It only matters when it’s tested. And out there?" He motioned vaguely towards the world beyond the walls. "The Federation will test every part of you. Your strength, your will, and your heart." Satisfied, Redbell allowed the faintest trace of a smirk to appear. "Rest up. You’re going to need that fire. The rebellion isn’t a place for the faint-hearted, but something tells me you’ll do just fine." He turned on his heel, leaving Rowan in the stillness of the surgical bay. He exhaled deeply, letting his body sink further into the bed. "That’s a pretty big heart you’ve got there," came a voice from the ride side of Rowan. Rowan’s head turned sharply to the right, his eyes landing on another patient lying on a bed a few meters away. The man looked younger, probably in his early twenties, with a casual demeanor that seemed to defy the reality they were in. His dark hair was messy, and he had a faint smirk that hinted at a devil-may-care attitude. "It would’ve sucked if the Federation got to take you away," the younger man continued, shifting slightly to get more comfortable. Rowan raised an eyebrow, his curiosity piqued. "And you are…?" The younger man gave a slight chuckle and extended a hand lazily in Rowan’s direction. "Name’s Kade. Just another guy who figured the rebellion’s a better choice than living under the Federation’s boot." Rowan hesitated before shaking the man’s hand. Kade’s grip was firm, yet his casual attitude made Rowan feel slightly at ease. "You’re not wrong," Rowan replied, his tone still cautious. "Don’t worry, man," Kade said, leaning back on his bed with a grin. "The rebellion’s rough, sure, but it’s better than being a mindless drone for the Federation. At least here, you get to fight for something real." Rowan studied him for a moment. "You sound like you’ve been through it. Were you a Federal soldier too?" Kade nodded, a shadow briefly crossing his features. "Yeah, I was. Not on the front lines, though. I was an engineer. Did hard breaching, fixed up vehicles, and patched up gear for the grunts." He smirked faintly. "Though, honestly? It sucked. Especially when they stopped paying us on time. Or at all." "Stopped paying you?" Rowan repeated, frowning. Kade shrugged. "Federation bureaucracy, man. They’re too busy lining their own pockets to give a damn about us. When I started asking questions, let’s just say… things got a little *heated.*" Rowan leaned forward slightly, intrigued. "So you joined the rebellion." "Didn’t have much of a choice," Kade admitted, his tone lighter now. "The alternative was… well, you know what the Federation does to people who don’t toe the line." He shuddered slightly, the memory clearly unpleasant. "But here? At least I get to put my skills to use without worrying about getting shoved in some detention center for asking the wrong questions. You ever noticed a guy that gave you a look? You you just.. Shook?" Rowan stared at Kade, as yes, that did happen. "Yeah, why..?" "That was a netrunner from this rebellion, they transfered that virus to us with just eye contact, its like a rat file but in your head." Kade sounded pretty serious, but yeah, it did make sense, though the process of a fake woman of your dreams just brings you to this place, is sickening. Rowan nodded, a faint smile tugging at the corner of his lips. "Also, hard breaching and vehicle repairs, huh? That’s a hell of a skill set." Kade grinned, his confidence returning. "What can I say? I like to keep busy. And hey, if you ever need someone to blow a hole in something or fix your ride, I’m your guy." Rowan chuckled softly, the first genuine laugh he’d had in what felt like years. "I’ll keep that in mind." "Good," Kade said with a wink. "Because something tells me we’re going to see a lot of action together." The room fell into a comfortable silence after that, Kade leaned back against his bed with a casual shrug, breaking the silence. “You know,” he began, his voice quieter than before, “I think we could be really good friends.” “Oh yeah? What makes you say that?” Kade chuckled, though there was a hint of melancholy in his tone. “Honestly? Nobody’s been willing to talk to me in this place. I mean, I get it, everyone’s got their own trauma, their own battles. And I figured… maybe it wouldn’t be worth trying anymore. But then I thought, screw it. One more time. One more person. And here you are.” Rowan nodded, the warmth of genuine connection settling in his chest. “Well, I’m glad you did. Let’s make it official then.” He extended a hand, and Kade grinned, shaking it firmly. “Friends,” Kade said with a nod, his expression softening. After a moment, Rowan tilted his head, curiosity in his eyes. “So, how’d you end up here anyway? What’s your story?” Kade’s grin fainted slightly, his eyes darkening with memories he had tried to bury. He leaned forward. “It’s… not a happy one,” he admitted. “Back when the Federation started their Midwest cleansing operation, my town was caught right in the middle of it. Guess we weren’t ‘useful’ enough for their vision of a perfect empire.” Rowan frowned, leaning in. “The cleansing operation?” “They wiped out entire towns, kept it hidden,” Kade said, his voice flat. “People who didn’t fit their mold. The elderly, the sick, the dissenters, gone. And anyone fit enough, like me? Conscripted. My family, though…” He trailed off.. “They tried to fight back. Tried to stop them from taking me. It didn’t end well.” Rowan’s heart sank. “Kade, I—” “Don’t,” Kade interrupted, shaking his head. “It’s not your fault. But yeah, they killed them. Right there in front of me. And then they threw me in the back of a transport like I was nothing. At the time, I didn’t even process it. Just… numb. But now? Now that I can remember it all, now that the vision of the Thunderhead’s control is gone?” He clenched his fists. “It hurts like hell.” Rowan took a deep breath, unsure what to say. “I’m sorry. No one should have to go through that.” Kade leaned back, his expression distant. “The Federation doesn’t care. They’ll ignore every ethical boundary to keep their machine of an empire running. They don’t see people, they see cogs in the system. Replaceable, expendable. Humanity’s enslaved, Rowan. Secretly, quietly. They’ve built this whole illusion of freedom, but it’s all a lie.” Rowan’s brow furrowed. “What do you mean, enslaved? The people… they don’t know?” Kade shook his head. “Most don’t. They think they’re free because they have jobs, families, lives. But the Federation controls everything, where they live, what they eat, what they believe. The Thunderhead doesn’t just monitor people, Rowan, it rewrites them. It’s like a leash around the mind, one you don’t even realize you’re wearing. And if anyone steps out of line, well…” He gestured vaguely, his meaning clear. Rowan felt a cold weight settle over him. “So, what’s the endgame then? What does the Federation want?” Kade’s look hardened. “Total control. No rebellions, no resistance, no individuality. Just a system that feeds itself. And the worst part? They’re basically winning. Hell, they’ve already won in some places.” “Well, they haven’t won here. Not yet. And as long as we’re still breathing, we’ve got a chance to stop them.” Kade looked at Rowan for a long moment, then smiled, a small, genuine thing. “You’ve got a big heart, you know that? The kind of heart they’d hate. Makes me glad you’re here, man. We’re gonna need it.” Rowan nodded, determination burning in his chest. “We’ll take them down. One way or another.” Kade smirked, a glimmer of hope lighting his eyes. “Damn right we will.” Kade glanced over at the digital clock mounted on the wall, its soft blue glow illuminating the otherwise dim room. “It’s getting late,” he said, leaning back on his bed with a stretch. “They’re gonna run us down tomorrow, y’know? All about what it means to join the rebellion, what they expect from us. They should, honestly. It’s not a choice to take lightly.” Rowan followed his eyes to the clock, noting the time. It was later than he’d realized. Kade continued, his voice soft but reassuring. “Don’t worry about anything tonight, though. This facility’s secure. Nobody’s getting in here, not without a hell of a fight. It’s okay to rest, Rowan. You’ve earned it.” Rowan nodded, exhaling a long breath as the tension in his shoulders eased. “Yeah,” he agreed quietly. “It’s late. I should try to sleep.” Kade gave him a small smile, adjusting his blanket. “Good idea. Tomorrow’s gonna be a big day. Don’t overthink it, just take it one step at a time.” Rowan settled back onto his cot, staring up at the faintly glowing ceiling lights as his eyelids grew heavier. For the first time in what felt like ages, he allowed himself to let go, if only for a little while. Sleep came slowly but surely, carrying him into the quiet promise of a new chapter.
